Preface
T
his thesis represents the culmination of the Master’s education in Civil Engineering,
and years of studying, at the Technical University of Denmark, and is written to fulﬁll
the graduation requirements of the program. It is the result of project work done by the
author, that have taken place in the period 01/05-2017 to 01/10-2017 and counts for 30
ECTS-points, the basis of which where to investigate the viability of wood ash and mine
tailings in concrete.
The thesis is written in such a way that other students at DTU’s Department of Civil
engineering, or any similar education elsewhere, can understand it and the related project
work. It is therefore expected that the reader possesses basic knowledge, regarding cement,
concrete and experimental tests conducted with such materials. However, a certain amount
of theory relevant to the project is included.
Calculations where done using a combination of Excel and Matlab, while the graphs con-
tained within, were made using Matlab alone. The thesis itself has been written using LATEX.
For reference management, the Vancouver reference style is used.
Enjoy your reading.
Benjamin A. R. Ebert

II
==PAGE:6==
Abstract
With an increased focus on sustainable development and a sustainable future, green engi-
neering calls for changes in the current engineering practises. Buildings and infrastructure
consume wast amounts of materials and resources, among which concrete and cement are
dominant. Cement production emits large amounts of carbon dioxide, accounting for 8% of
the global emissions per year. Cement and concrete are therefore a key areas of focus, in
order to include the use of secondary resources.
Such secondary resources could be the commercially worthless tailings from ore mining or
the leftover residue ash from combined heat and power plants, that burns biomass in the
form of wood.
The goal of this thesis has been to study the properties of concrete with alternative additives
such as wood ash and tailing additives and analyse their eﬀectiveness. This is accomplished
by measuring the compressive strength at diﬀerent point of curing, for samples where 5 and
10% of the cement has been replaced by additives. Instead of making concrete at a large
scale, mortar samples were used. Together with the compression testing, thermogravimetric
analysis was done on crushed cement paste samples with the same replacement ratios. From
the thermogravimetric analysis the Ca(OH)2 content were measured. Finally the loss of
compressive strength and workability with increasing substitution amount is analysed. The
results are compared to a control group consisting of samples without additives and samples
with coal ﬂy ash and silica fume.
Two types of wood ash and two types of tailings were studied. The wood ash comes from
Amagerværkert and Køge Kraftvarmeværk, while the tailings are from the swedish mine
Zinkgruvan and the former gold mine Nalunaq in greenland.
The analyse showed that the use of wood ash and tailings did not increase the compressive
strength over time, as coal ﬂy ash and silica fume did. An increase in substitution amount
decreased the strength further, contrary to the eﬀect of coal ﬂy ash and silica fume. The
thermogravimetric analysis indicated no decrease in Ca(OH)2 amount. Coupled with the
compressive testing, it was surmised that no pozzolanic reactions occur when adding wood
ash or tailings. It was demonstrated that an increase in tailing substitution lowered the
compressive strength and workability of the mortar. The decrease is not signiﬁcantly large.
Increased substitution with wood ash, signiﬁcantly decreased both the compressive strength
and the workability.
It was concluded that substitution with wood ash and tailings does not strengthen the
properties of concrete.
Keywords:
Concrete, Compression strength, Workability, Thermogravimetric analysis,
Wood ash, Mine tailings

Sammenfatning
Med et øget fokus på den bæredygtige udvikling og en bæredygtig fremtid, er der brug for at
bringe den bæredygtige omstilling ind i ingeniørfaget. Store mængder materiale og resurser
bliver brugt når der skal opføres bygninger og laves ny infrastruktur. Her er det beton og
cement der er dominerende. Produktionen af cement udleder store mængder carbon dioxid.
Cementproduktionen alene står for 8% af den årlige globale udledning. Cement og beton er
derfor hovedfokus, når det gælder om at inkludere sekundære resurser.
De sekundære resurser kan bl.a. være det kommercielt værdiløse aﬀald fra minedrift eller
rester af aske fra kraftvarmeværker der forbrænder biomasse i form af træ.
Målet med specialet har været at studere betonens egenskaber, når det er tilført alternative
tilsætningsstoﬀer, såsom træaske og mineaﬀald, og på den måde analysere tilsætningsstof-
fernes eﬀekt. Dette er opnået ved at måle trykstyrken på forskellige tidspunkter i hærd-
ningsprocessen. Prøver hvor 5 og 10% af cementen blev udskiftet med tilsætningsstoﬀer
blev benyttet. I stedet for at lave store mængder beton, blev mørtel brugt til forsøgene. En
thermogravimetrisk analyse foretoges på prøver af knust cementpasta med samme udskift-
ningsratio, samtidig med at der blev fortaget trykstyrketests. Ved den thermogravimetriske
analyse blev Ca(OH)2 målt. Til sidst blev tabet af trykstyrke og bearbejdelighed kontra den
øgede substitutionsmængde analyseret. Resultatet sammenlignedes med en kontrolgruppe
bestående af prøver uden tilsætningsstoﬀer og prøver med kulﬂyveaske og mikrosilica.
To typer træaske og to typer mineaﬀald studeredes. Træasken kommer fra Amagerværket og
Køge Kraftvarmeværk, mens mineaﬀaldet kommer fra den svenske mine Zinkgruvan og den
tidligere guldmine Nalunaq i Grønland. Analyserne viste at brugen af træaske og mineaf-
fald ikke øgede trykstyrken over tid, på samme måde som kulﬂyveasken og mikrosilicaen
gjorde det. En forøgelse af substitutionsmængden formindskede styrken yderligere, modsat
den observerede eﬀekt af kulﬂyveaske og mikrosilica. Den thermogravimetriske analyse op-
fangede ikke nogen nedgang af mængden af Ca(OH)2. Sammenholdt med trykstyrketesten
udledtes det, at der ikke opstod nogen puzzolansk reaktion når der tilførtes træaske eller
mineaﬀald. Det blev derimod bevist at en forøgelse af mineaﬀfaldssubstitutionsmængden
nedsatte mørtlens trykstyrke og bearbejdedelighed. Nedsættelsen af bearbejdeligheden er
ikke stor nok til at være signiﬁkant. En forøgelse af træaske som substitution nedsatte til
gengæld trykstyrken og bearbejdeligheden signiﬁkant.
Det konkluderes at substitution med træaske og mineaﬀald ikke styrker betonens egenskaber.
Nøgleord: Beton, trykstyrke, bearbejdelighed, thermogravimetrisk analyse, træaske, mineaf-
fald.

1
Introduction
T
he goal of sustainable development and a sustainable future, has since its conception
gained substantial traction, with an increased focus on the environment, technology
and green engineering today, than previously. According to Balaguru et al. [1], green en-
gineering is described as a much needed approach, changing current engineering disciplines
and practises to those that promote sustainability. The guiding principles of green engi-
neering, includes waste minimization, life cycle thinking, as well as the use of biodegradable
materials in building structures. Green engineering, thus involves recycling and research
into the replacement of natural raw materials with secondary resources.
Buildings and infrastructure consume a wast amount of materials, the most common being
concrete, steel, asphalt and clay. Nearly every country in the world has the knowledge and
materials available to produce concrete, as well as the expertise to include it in designs and
constructions, it is therefore sladed to be the dominant material used in the near future [1].
The most important ingredient in concrete is portland cement, with 12 billion ton produced
yearly [2]. As cement consists of easy to obtain chemical elements, as well as being simple
and inexpensive to produce, the high production rate is understandable.
A major drawback of cement however, is the resulting emissions of carbon dioxide. The
emissions of CO2 are twofold. Carbon dioxide is emitted during carbonate oxidation in the
production process, which contributed with 4.0% of the global emissions in 2015 [3]. Sec-
ondly, emissions occur from the fuel consumed during production, thus making the emissions
from cement production roughly 8.0% of the annual discharge [3]. Cement and concrete is
therefore a key area of focus, when it comes to replacement with secondary resources and
recycling.
One such possible resource is ﬂy ash from combined heat and power (CHP) plants fuelled
by biomass. In Denmark it has been tradition to use coal as a fuel source, and the resulting
ﬂy ash, as a cement replacement in concrete. However, with the new Copenhagen 2025
climate plan, all power, heat and cooling, in the Danish capital, will primarily be based on
wind, biomass, geothermal and waste [4]. Several Danish CHP plants are already utilizing
biomass as a fuel source, like Køge kraftvarmeværk and Amagerværket, the leftover ﬂy ash
from combustion is nevertheless underutilized. Currently the ash is either used, when envi-
ronment approved, as landﬁll or as ﬁlling for ski slopes. Research is under way, attempting
to properly utilize biomass ash, as fertilizer. It would however be beneﬁcial if biomass ﬂy
ash could replace the future void left behind by coal ﬂy ash.
1
==PAGE:15==
1. Introduction
Another potential resource is mine tailings, leftover from mineral processing and coal wash-
ing after mining. Mineral processing is comprised of size reduction and separation of the
diﬀerent minerals at mills, in contrast to coal that are washed, creating a slurry of waste
[5]. Processing and washing produces two things. The ﬁrst being an accumulated supply
of the material mined for, and secondly the residual waste known as tailings, comprised of
either solids or liquids. It is estimated that after processing, depending on the ore mined,
that up to and more then 99% of the originally mined material ends up as tailings. As
metals are frequently mined from the earth, large amounts of tailings are generated that
end up in speciﬁc storage facilities, such as tailing dams and are otherwise heavily underused.
The aim of this thesis is to investigate the viability of using ﬂy ash from biomass, in the
form of wood, and mine tailings as cement replacement for making concrete. This is accom-
plished by analysing the change in compressive strength over time, as well as the change in
strength with replacement amount. Additionally the diﬀerences in workability, the higher
the replacement amount are evaluated concurrently. Furthermore samples of cement paste
are tested using thermogravimetry in order to further determine the eﬀectiveness of wood
ash and tailings with cement. Two types of ﬂy ash and mine tailings have been acquired.
The wood ash comes from Køge Kraftvarmeværk and Amagerværket, while the tailings are
from the Swedish mine Zinkgruvan and the former gold mine Nalunaq in Greenland. To
asses their eﬀectiveness the ﬂy ashes and tailings are tested against a control group consist-
ing of reference samples of pure cement and samples prepared with coal ﬂy ash and silica
fume replacement.

2
Theory
C

2.1 |
Knowledge Gaps
Researching the potential usages of wood ash and mine tailings is not a new prospect, with
research on the subject dating back several years. A lot of knowledge on the topic has thus
been accumulated. However, several knowledge gaps are identiﬁable. Table 2.1.1 includes an
excerpt of literature about using wood ash and mine tailings as a cement replacement. The
articles were chosen, based on their similarities with the topic of this thesis. The articles
were found using the Technical University of Denmark’s online database DTU Findit.
From the table and precipitating literature search, it became clear, that research concerning
cement replacement with mine tailings, are lacking compared to replacement with wood
ash, making it a prime area for more research. When searching for mine tailings in conjunc-
tion with cement, the majority of the results concerns tailing stabilization and backﬁlling.
Other results did focus on concrete with mine tailings, but with a diﬀerent focus, deemed
irrelevant. Examples include studies into minimizing the shrinkage of concrete using tailings.
It is evident, that a lot of research focus has been on characterising the diﬀerent ashes
and tailings used, measuring the mechanical strength of the cement with replacement, as
well as the workability and durability. The ashes and tailings used varies greatly, requiring
a characterisation and understanding their eﬀects on the workability. Nevertheless, other
ways of assessing the eﬀect of wood ash and tailing additives exist, other than measuring
the material strength.
The products of cement hydration can be determined by utilizing thermogravimetric anal-
ysis (TGA) and x-ray diﬀraction (XRD), as discussed in the articles by Zhihua et al. [6]
and Lilkov et al. [7]. The latter of the two methods are however, not utilized as part of this
project. By comparing the mechanical strength with the cement hydration, at diﬀerent ages
of curing, a better understanding of the admixtures eﬀects can be achieved. Of the 9 articles
shown in Table 2.1.1, [8] alone utilizes thermogravimetric analysis to better understand the
eﬀectiveness of tailings in conjunction with cement. This is done by measuring the amount
of Ca(OH)2 in prepared samples, with and without tailings. In the article by Lilkov et al.
[7], it is measured with samples containing ﬂy ash.As part of this project the amount of

2. Theory
2.2 |
Wood ash
Wood ash is the by-product of wood incineration. The terminology covers a large range of
incineration types, for this thesis the residue ash accumulating from thermal combustion of
wood biomass for heat and electricity production is investigated. However, comparisons are
done with ash from other types of incinerations.
2.2.1
Production
Combustion of biomass and thereby wood, is among the dominant bio energy applications,
with increasing amounts of biomass based power plants around the world [17], as well as
in the capital of Denmark [4]. This requires large amounts of wood based fuels. Wood
based fuels can come from two sources. Short rotation forestry, where fast growing species
are cultivated on agricultural land and forest biomass, with the latter being the prevalent
source [17]. Fuel from forest biomass comes in four types [18], traditional ﬁrewood, primary
residues, secondary residues and tertiary residues.
Primary residues stem from logging
operations, while secondary residues are by-products of industry, such as shavings and chips.
The tertiary residues are used wood from construction, demolition and packaging.
The forest fuels consists of organic compounds synthesised by the trees as part of their
photosynthesis. The majority of the compound elements are carbon (C), oxygen (O) and
hydrogen (H), with nitrogen (N) and sulphur(S) in smaller amounts. During incineration,
chemical energy stored in the chemical bonds are released as heat energy through oxida-
tion. The bulk of the organic matter is volatilised as CO2 and H2O, with N and S being
volatilised as NOx and SO2 respectively. Wood based fuel also contain other elements that
are volatilised at higher temperatures, and are left in the incineration bed as bottom ash, or
are volatilised and then quickly recondensed again as ﬂy ash. Depending on the fuel type,
between 2 to 20% of the original mass are left as ash [17]. According to [18] around 3 wt%
of the forest fuels are left as ash after combustion.
2.2.2
Composition and Characteristics
The resulting chemical composition and physical characteristics, are dependent on the plant
species, as well as the origin and parts of the plants used during incineration, [17]. The
incineration process and storage conditions also aﬀect the residue ash.
Altough the majority of the organic matter is oxidised or emitted as gaseous compounds
during incineration, organic matter can still be present in the ash as charred coal, due
to incomplete combustion. Other elements present in the wood, are retained in the ash.
The major elements left in the ash are calcium (Ca), potassium (K), magnesium (Mg),
silicon (Si), aluminium (A), iron (Fe), phosphorus (P), sodium (Na), manganese (Mn)
and sulphur (S).
The greater part of the major elements are in the form of oxides or
hydroxides [18].
The mineralogy is however described as complex with a wide range of
minerals possible present. Furthermore, K is commonly found in salt with a high solubility.
Table 2.2.1 shows the potential amount of major elements present in wood ﬂy ash.
5

Trace elements found in wood ash are zinc (Zn), arsenic (As), nickel (Ni), chromium (Cr),
lead (Pb), mercury (Hg), copper (Cu), boron (B), molybdenum (Mo), vanadium (V ), bar-
ium (Ba), cadmium (Cd) and silver (Ag) [18]. The amounts of K, S, B, Na and Cu are
reported to decrease with incineration temperature, while Mg, P, Mn, Al, Fe, Si and Ca
remains unaﬀected. The amount of heavy metals in wood ash is lower compared to coal
ash, the alkalinity is however, higher in wood ash [17]. During incineration heavy metals
accumulate in the ﬂy ash, with the exception of Zn [17]. Wood ash has an alkaline reaction
and when mixed with water achieves an high pH [18].
Wood ash may also include organic pollutants such as polychorinated dibenzodioxin, biphenyls,
dibenzofuran and polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons.
The properties of the ash is considered to change during storage, as CO2 and moisture
from the air spontaneously reacts with the ash, forming hydroxides, carbonates, bicarbon-
ates and other minerals such as gypsum and ettringite [18]. The reactivity of the resulting
ash is reduced, by the formation of larger agglomerates and stabler, denser structures, along
with the solubility.
2.2.3
Disposal and Areas of Application
Wood ash is commonly disposed of in landﬁlls, however potential areas of application are as
fertilisers, returning nutrients to forest ecosystems and for geotechnical constructions and
industrial processes.

==PAGE:20==
2. Theory
2.3 |
Mine tailing
Reﬁnement of mined hard rock metal ores and industrial mineral deposit comprises a size
reduction of the original material and separation of the economic product from the gangue
(commercially worthless material).
However, only a small percentage of the mined rock
contains the valuable minerals sought after. The end products of the ore reﬁnement are
therefore a concentrated amount of the valuable minerals otherwise known as the economic
product and residual waste known as tailings. Tailings therefore consists of ground rock and
process eﬄuents. Tailings refer to both the solid and liquid waste.
2.3.1
Production
Ore processing is divided into two stages. In the ﬁrst stage, the mined blocks of hard rock
ores are crushed, ground and milled, breaking down the ore, freeing the valuable material
from the gangue. Crushing of the ore is a dry process, opposite crushing, grinding is gener-
ally a wet process, involving abrasion of the particles. The second stage consists of removing
the economic product from the crushed gangue. This is done using several diﬀerent meth-
ods, that utilize the varying gravimetric, magnetic, electrical and surface properties of the
ore and gangue, separating the two. Many of the ore recovery operations process the ore in
water, exploiting ground and surface water as process water. Hydro metallurgical process-
ing requires speciﬁc chemicals, referred to as process chemicals, in order to extract the ore.
Such chemicals are classiﬁed as ﬂotation reagents, modiﬁers, ﬂocculants/coagulants, hydro
metallurgical agents and oxidants [5].
The diﬀerent methods are never 100% eﬀective, full recovery of the economic product is
therefore not possible. Vast amounts of rock are processed this way, albeit if low grade
metal ores are processed, more than 99% of the mined material may end up as tailings.
Tailings are typically produced in the form of a ﬁne-grained sediment-water slurry. Tailings,
therefore consists of solids and liquids.
The solids are removed together with the spent
process water, and stored in a designated management or storage facility.
2.3.2
Composition and Characteristics
Due to its production, tailings vary considerably in its chemical composition and physical
properties. According to [5], the grain size of tailings is relatively restricted, ranging from
clay size (2 µm) to sand size (2 mm). Typically, dried tailings consist of 70-80 wt.% sand
sized particles and 20-30 wt.% clay sized particles. The resulting grain size of the tailings
is dependent on the mined ore and its gangue phases, as well as the applied crushing and
grinding techniques, changing from mine to mine.
The tailing solids are made up of the unwanted minerals in the gangue phase. These usually
consist of silicates, oxides, hydroxides, carbonates and sulﬁdes [5]. Furthermore, the recovery
of the economic product is never 100% eﬀective.
It is therefore often assumed that the
tailings contain small amounts of the economic product, albeit with a microscopic grain
size.
Numerous fractions of the processed chemicals may ultimately ﬁnd its way into the tailings.
7
==PAGE:21==
2. Theory
Tailing liquids frequently contain some levels of organic chemicals, cyanide, sulphuric acid
and other reagents used during mineral recovery.
When deposited in repositories, tailings are subject to change over time. The solids and
liquids react, forming an equilibrium.
It is stated by Lottermoser [5], that the tailings
undergo a form of diagenesis when stored. Additionally, tailings are subjected to physi-
cal and biological changes, such as compaction, cementation as well as mineral dissolution
and recrystallisation. Tailings can therefore consist of the primary ore and gangue materi-
als, secondary minerals formed during weathering and chemical precipitates formed during
processing and after disposal.
2.3.3
Disposal and Areas of Application
As designated waste, mine tailings do not have any speciﬁc usage, and are therefore disposed
of. Most of the tailings produced worldwide are pumped into tailings management facilities
or tailings storage facilities. A common method of storage is tailing dams. Tailing dams are
build like conventional dams, and are described as purpose-built sedimentation ponds where
the residue waste and process water are stored [5]. Tailing dams are prone to failure, with an
average failure of 1 per year, in the last thirty years, causing casualties and contamination.
Other methods of disposal include backﬁlling, where the tailings are pumped underground
or into mined-out open pits and marine disposal, where the tailings are disposed of deep in
the ocean.
Although no speciﬁc usage exits, tailings can be recycled and reused. Manganese tailings may
be used in agro forestry and as coatings, and base metal tailings and low grade metal ores may
be planted with suitable plant species, capable of extracting the metals. Additionally tailings
represent a potential source for carbon dioxide sequestration technology [5]. Furthermore,
the European Commission encourages the use of tailings as aggregates [19].

==PAGE:22==
2. Theory
2.4 |
Portland Cement
Portland cement is a binder material, that when mixed with water creates cement paste,
crucial to making concrete. The binder properties are closely related to the chemical compo-
sition and ﬁneness of the cement. It was invented and patented by Joseph Aspdin in 1824.
The name Portland comes from the colour of cement, which is reminiscent of the colour of
Portland stone, a construction stone from the isle of Portland. The therm cement comes
from the Latin word cementum, meaning cut stone.
It was used for the ﬁrst time in Denmark, in 1852, when making the foundation for Langebro
in Copenhagen [20].
However the utilization of a binder material for construction was
common before the creation of Portland cement. The people of Etruria, used slaked lime as
a binder, while the Romans later used burned lime and volcanic ash, eventually creating a
precursor cement to Portland cement.
2.4.1
Raw Materials
During cement production, the chemical composition of the cement is controlled by charac-
teristic key values, based on the amount of calcium, silicon, aluminium and iron in the raw
materials used. The end cement product is controlled by strict bounds, however, this is not
the case for the raw materials, as long as the proportions of the raw mixture are correct. This
is due to the high temperatures used during production, burning away many of the chemical
components in the raw materials [21]. Raw materials used for cement manufacturing around
the globe are shown in Table 2.4.1.
Tabel 2.4.1: Raw materials used for cement manufacturing. From [21].

2. Theory
2.4.2
Manufacturing Process
All types of Portland cement are in principal made the same way, albeit with the mentioned
variations in the raw mix. The manufacturing process can be divided into two phases
1. Manufacturing Portland cement clinker.
2. Pulverizing the clinker to the desired particle size.
Their are three methods for manufacturing Portland cement clinker
• The wet method
• The dry method
• The semi-wet method
To achieve the desired chemical composition in the cement clinker, the raw-mix is homog-
enized by crushing, grinding and blending the mix. Aluminium, iron and magnesium ions,
together with alkalies, are part of the raw mix to facilitate clinker formation at a lower
temperature. The presence of these materials has a mineralizing eﬀect on the formation of
calcium silicates.
The wet method is characterized by mixing and pre-processing the raw materials in wa-
ter. The ﬁnished raw mix is therefore available as a slurry. Using the dry method the raw
materials are if necessary dried ﬁrst, then pre-processed and mixed. The ﬁnished raw mix is
then available as a dry powder. The semi dry method consists of mixing the raw materials
like the wet method followed by drying.
The manufacturing process is essentially a long process, that wont be explained in depth as
part of this project. In essence, the raw mix is heated in a calcinator at approx. 900°C in
order to separate carbon dioxide from the raw mix. Thereafter, the heated powder is led
to a rotary oven, wherein the materials are heated to between 1400°C and 1500°C. During
the burning, partial melting and sintering occur forming the clinker minerals. According to
Metha et al. [22] the chemical reactions taking place during manufacturing can be expressed
as:
Limestone

The ﬁnal phase in the manufacturing process, is pulverizing the clinker to an average particle
between 10 and 15 µm. This is done with ball mills. At this stage 5 % gypsum or calcium
sulphate is crushed together with the cement clinker.

and alkalis.

The formed C −S −H gel is the key strength giving component in hardened cement paste,

however it is also responsible for a fast strength development due to its quick reaction with
water. C3S hydration is to some degree aﬀected by the other cement components. Both

the hydration. C3S hydration has a moderate to high heat development, approximately 500

Belites rate of hydration is as mentioned substantially lower then alite. According to Kur-
dowski [23], after 24 hours of hydration, the surface of belite particles are similar to alites
after 5 minutes of hydration. This is only the case for pure phases. The quicker hydration
of alite serves as an accelerator for belite. Although alite and belite hydrate at diﬀerent
rates, it is a commonly accepted opinion, that the mechanism of reaction is the same [23].
Characteristic of C2S is therefore a slow reaction and high end strength, as well as low heat
development. At complete hydration the heat development is approx. 210 j
g [24]. The wa-
ter consumption of belite is of a similar magnitude as alite, with 21 wt.% chemically bound
water and 15% physically absorbed water.
C3A
Portland cement generally consists of 5-12 wt.% aluminate, however certain speciality ce-
ments, like sulphate resistant cement, has between 0-5 % aluminate. Unlike alite and belite,
Aluminate reacts with both water and gypsum. The addition of gypsum retards aluminates
reaction with water, that otherwise would react quickly, hardening within minutes [20].
The quick reaction between aluminate and water is, according to Kurdowski [23]:

C4AF
The ﬁnal clinker mineral in Portland cement is ferrite. Compared to the other 3 minerals,
ferrite has a small contribution to the strength development. It reacts in the same way
as aluminate, ﬁrst forming metastable hydrates, eventually forming stable end products.
The reactions are in comparison slower, with a lower heat generation of 300 j
g at complete
hydration. The water consumed, depending on the reaction products formed, is between
37-70 wt.%.
Gypsum
As mentioned, gypsum works as a regulator for the cement hydration reactions, predom-
inantly aluminates reaction with water.
The retarding eﬀect is due to the formation of
ettringite on the surface of the aluminate particles, inhibiting further hydration, changing
the setting time.

The ettringite is stable as long as sulphate is available. However, when the sulphate depletion
point is reached, ettringite starts reacting with the aluminate forming monosulphate. The
hydration of ferrite, follows the same reactions as for aluminate, forming C3(A, F)·3C ¯S·H32
and 3(C3(A, F) · C ¯S · H12).
Other than inhibiting hydration, gypsum has a positive eﬀect on the early age strength
development and reduces the volume changes, with varying humidity.

2. Theory
Calcium oxide
During the manufacturing process the majority of the CaO is bound to the oxides SiO2,
Al2O3 and Fe2O3, forming the clinker minerals. The unbound CaO is referred to as free
lime, reacting with water forming Ca(OH)2. If the amount of free lime is high with a large
particle size, the reaction can lead to expansions during cement hydration. In Danish cement,
the amount of free lime is between 0.5- 2 wt%, posing no risk of expansion [24].
Magnesium oxide
MgO is chemically related to CaO, it is however, not bound in the same way, during clinker
formation. It is estimated that 1% of the MgO is bound in the cement clinkers [24]. The
remaining MgO is burned, forming crystalline MgO, slowly reacting with water, forming
Mg(OH)2. Like CaO, the reaction is expansive.
Alkalis
The alkalis in the cement are sodium oxide Na2O and potassium oxide K2O. They are found
as part of the clinker minerals, replacing CaO and partially as soluble alkali sulphates.The
alkali content of a cement is usually transformed into an equivalent Na2Oeq wt% content.
The equation is given as:
Na2Oeq(%) = Na2Owt% + 0.658 · K2Owt%
(2.4.1)
Where 0.658 is a conversion factor, reﬂecting the diﬀerences in molecular weight between
the two.
The equivalent alkali content varies with each cement type, and are typically found in small
amounts. Although the amount of alkalis are small, they appear to still have an eﬀect on the
hydration scheme. A higher amount of alkalis accelerate the earlier strength development,
resulting in a lower ﬁnal strength [20].
If concrete is made with aggregate containing amorphous silica, it can react with the al-
kali content, forming alkali silica gel. The reaction is expansive, causing cracks.
2.4.4
Fineness
The ﬁneness of cement inﬂuences the compactness and chemical reactions, and is therefore
an important factor. The electrostatic surface tension that hampers free packing of the
particles, is greater the higher the ﬁneness. A ﬁne cement will therefore, have a looser com-
paction and by implication a higher bulk density. When mixed with water the same surface
tension aﬀects the particles movement increasing the amount of water to achieve a certain
consistency.
The chemical reactions, transpiring during cement hydration, occurs quicker the ﬁner milled
the cement is. The strength develops faster, however the diﬀerence in strength compared
14
==PAGE:28==
2. Theory
to a crude cement decreases over time. A decreased reaction time also increases the heat
developed.
2.4.5
Hydration
The reaction between cement and water is described as a highly complex process [23] and
therefore, will not be presented in full detail as part of this thesis, beyond the review of
the hydration reactions already presented and subsequent review of the cement hydration
stages. As mentioned in Section 2.4.3, Portland cement is primarily made up of 4 phases.
Each phase hydrates diﬀerently, forming diﬀerent products of hydration. Figure 2.4.1 depicts
the diﬀerent solidiﬁed products of hydration, at diﬀerent ages, and their relative amount.
The ﬁgure depicts how the ﬁrst hydrate to crystallize is the ettringite, followed by the
formation of portlandite, and a vast increase in C −S −H gel. The ﬁgure depicts the
sulphate depletion point, with a decrease in ettringite and rise in monosulphate.
Figure 2.4.1: Hydration age and relative amount of hydration products. From [26].
As previously mentioned, the major component in Portland cement is silicates, with a small
amount of aluminates, the diﬀerent stages of cement hydration therefore closely follows that
of alite hydration [25]. The hydration of cement can therefore be separated into ﬁve stages,
distinguishable on a calorimetric curve, see Figure 2.4.2. According to Kurdowski [23], the
ﬁve stages are referred to as the initial dissolution or pre-induction, induction period, hy-
dration rate increase, hydration rate decrease and low hydration rate.
According to Marchon et al.
[25], shortly after water is added, the diﬀerent anhydrous
phases dissolve. The dissolution of the diﬀerent phases are highly exothermic creating the
ﬁrst peak. This period last only for a few minutes, but as previously stated, it is during the
ﬁrst few minutes that ettringite forms due to the high reactivity of aluminate. Following the
dissolution is a sudden deceleration and the induction period, with low reactivity.
15
==PAGE:29==
2. Theory
Figure 2.4.2: Schematic calorimetric curve, showing the diﬀerent stages of cement hydra-
tion. From [22].
The occurrence of the ﬁrst deceleration is a highly discussed subject, with several hypothesis,
describing how it works. One hypothesis involves the formation of a protective membrane
of hydrates on the particle surface, preventing further dissolution [25]. Where the surface
layer consists of metastable hydrates, that would either convert into more permeable state
hydrates or break due to osmotic pressure, marking the end of the induction period, see
Figure 2.4.3.
Figure 2.4.3: The Shceme of Cement hydration: a) Grains in water. b) Gel layer forma-
tion. c) Breaking of gel layer. d) Filling of pores. From [25].
Another theory is based on crystal dissolution from geochemistry. It implies that the dis-
solution mechanisms depend on the solution concentrations and therefore the degree of
saturation [25]. During the induction stage, C −S −H are still formed and upon reach-
ing a certain critical size, starts to grow, marking the beginning of the increased hydration
period.
The second peak seen in Figure 2.4.2 corresponds to the precipitation of the reaction prod-
ucts from silicate hydration, with the increased heat mainly stemming from the simultaneous
increase in alite dissolution. This stage is followed by a second deceleration, where a third
peak is visible. This peak represents the discussed sulphate depletion. The ﬁnal stage is
16
==PAGE:30==
2. Theory
described as a period of low reactivity, due to the slow diﬀusion of species in the hardened
material [25].
2.4.5.1
Accelerators and Retarders
Several compounds, both inorganic and organic, are capable of accelerating or retarding,
the hydration of cement.
Inorganic accelerators can be classiﬁed as either chlorides, sodium and potassium salts,
and nitrides and nitrates [23]. Chlorides are commonly applied accelerators, with CaCl2
improving the rate of hydration and heat evolution, enabling low temperature work [23].
Compared to the retarders, the number of organic accelerators are fewer. Among them are
triethanoloamine and sodium or calcium formate, as well as caboxyl acids, formaldehyde,
cationic polyesters, phenol resins and epoxy resins.
Calcium carbonate has been found to, likewise work as an accelerator.
In the presence
of CaCO3 the hydration of C3S is accelerated, with a shortened induction period. This is
also the case for sodium and potassium carbonates, that strongly accelerate C3S hydration.
The method of their acceleration is not fully understood [23]. Potassium and sodium sul-
phates are strong accelerators of hardening, without aﬀecting the induction period or the
setting time.
Many inorganic and organic retarders exist, the most commonly used being acids and their
salts, primarely boric, phosphates, hydroﬂoric, chromic and arsenic [23]. Additionally zinc
and lead oxides, hydroxides and salts have retarding capabilities. Some of the strongest re-
tarders include Na3PO4, Na2B4O7, Na3AsO4, NaF as well as Ca(H2PO4)2 · H2O.
17
==PAGE:31==
2. Theory
2.4.6
Pozzolanic Reaction
DS/EN 197-1:2012 [27] deﬁnes pozzolanic materials as natural materials of siliceous or silico-
aluminous composition or a combination of the two. Pozzolanic materials are not capable of
hydration when mixed with water. However, when ﬁnely ground and in contact with water,
they are capable of reacting with dissolved Ca(OH)2, forming the strength developing cal-
cium silicate and calcium aluminate compounds. Pozzolanas consists of reactive SiO2 and
Al2O3 as well as Fe2O3 and other oxides [27].
The pozzolanic reaction between a pozzolanic material, Ca(OH)2 ,and water is given as
according to Metha et al. [22]:
2SiO2
+
3Ca(OH)2
+
H2O
→
3CaO · 2SiO2 · 3H2O
2S
+
3CH
+
H
→
C3S2H6
Pozzolan
+
Portlandite
+
Water
→
C −S −Hgel
The pozzolanic reaction is slow, the rates of heat liberation and strength development are
therefore slow as well, contributing to the later strength development. Figure 2.4.4 depicts
the strength development of cement with 10%, 20% and 30% Greek natural mineral pozzolan
at diﬀerent ages. Furthermore, because the reaction consumes Ca(OH)2, the addition of
pozzolanic materials changes the durability.
Figure 2.4.4: Compressive strength of cement with a Greek natural mineral pozzolan. From
[22].
Two commonly used pozzolans are ﬂy ash from coal combustion and silica fume from the
silicon metal and ferro-silicon alloy industry.
18
==PAGE:32==
2. Theory
2.4.6.1
Coal Fly Ash
Coal ﬂy ash, like wood ash, is the by-product of combustion at CHP plants. However, coal
ﬂy ash is the result of coal passing through the high temperature zone of the furnace, where
the volatile matter and carbon is burned away [22]. Leftover are the mineral impurities,
like clays, quarts and feldspar, that melt and fuse together at high temperatures. The fused
matter are carried to the low temperature zones, where it solidiﬁes as spherical particles of
glass. As for wood ash, the minerals can agglomerate and form bottom ash, while the ﬁner
particles are carried by the ﬂue gas as ﬂy ash. The majority of the ﬂy ash particles consists
of solid spheres of glass. Hollow spheres known as cenospheres can occur [22].
According to Metha et al. [22], ﬂy ash can be divided into two categories based on their
mineral composition. The two categories are referred to as low calcium, containing less than
15 % analytical CaO and high calcium ﬂy ash with 15-45% analytical CaO.
Low calcium ﬂy ash, principally consists of aluminosilicate glasses, due to its higher pro-
portions of silica and aluminium. Sillimante Al2O3 · SiO2 or mulite (3Al2O3 · 2SiO2) can
crystallize as slender needles inside the glass spheres, because of insuﬃcient cooling. X-ray
diﬀraction have shown that the principal crystalline minerals in low calcium ﬂy ash are
quartz, mulite with either or both hematite and magnetite [22]. The crystalline minerals are
non reactive at standard temperatures, lowering the pozzolanic activity of the ash in large
amounts.
In comparison high calcium ﬂy ash, is considered more reactive, with more crystalline ma-
terials containing calcium, such as C3A, C ¯S and C4A3 ¯S. The calcium ions further increases
the reactivity of the aluminosilicate glass as well [22].
In general, most ﬂy ashes contain approx.
60-85% glass, 10-30% crystalline compounds
and up to 5% unburned carbon. The particle size distribution of a typical ﬂy ash ranges
from less then 1 µm to 100 µm.
The addition of ﬂy ash improves the workability of freshly mixed concrete by reducing
the size and volume of voids. According to Metha et al. [22], the small size and glassy
texture of ﬂy ash, reduces the amount of water required for a given consistency.
2.4.6.2
Silica Fume
Silica Fume is a by product of the silicon metal and ferrosilicon alloy industries, consisting of
amorphous silica. In induction arc furnaces, quartz are reduced to silicon at up to 2000°C,
producing SiO vapors, that oxidize an condenses at low temperatures as microscopic parti-
cles. The condensed material is then ﬁltered from the gasses.
Sillica fume is extremely pozzolanic, but is at the same time hard to handle, lowering the
water requirements, and by implication the workability [22]. Water-reducing admixtures,
such as superplastisisers, are therefore required. Silica fume contains between 85-90% amor-
19
==PAGE:33==
2. Theory
phous silica, with an average diameter of 0.1 µm. The particle distribution of silica fume is
two orders of magnitude ﬁner than Portland cement and coal ﬂy ash.
2.4.7
The Eﬀect of Filler
According to Moosber-Bustnes et al. [28], ﬁllers are materials whose function in concrete
is based mainly on the size and shape. Fillers are capable of interacting with cement in
diﬀerent ways, improving the particle packing and giving the fresh concrete other properties,
including the reduction of cement without loss of strength. Additionally Moosber-Bustnes
et al [28] deﬁnes ﬁllers as particles with a maximum size of 125 microns. The addition of
ﬁller can aﬀect concrete in three ways.
• On the physical level - the addition of ﬁne particles ﬁlls the intergranular voids between
the cement particles, improving the compactness of the concrete.
• On the surface chemical level - the added particles enhances the hydration by acting
as nucleation sites, integrating as part of the cement paste.
• On the chemical level - the particles react with a cements components, such as the
pozzolanic reaction.
2.5 |
Mortar and concrete
Mortar is the combination of cement, water and ﬁne aggregates, in the form of sand, while
concrete is the combination of cement, water, ﬁne aggregates and coarse aggregates in the
form of gravel.
As part of this thesis, mortar specimens are used for testing, but the
same concepts, that apply to concrete, apply to mortar. Therefore, sections discussing the
workability and strength development are included in the following.
2.5.1
Workability
As stated in Concrete[22], workability is speciﬁed as a property that determines the eﬀort
required to manipulate freshly mixed concrete with minimal loss of homogeneity. Workabil-
ity is governed by two concepts, the consistency and the stability.
The consistency, is a simple index, describing the mobility or ﬂow ability of the concrete or
otherwise the ease at which the fresh mixture ﬂows [22].
Stability, concerns the water holding capacity and coarse aggregate holding capacity of a
plastic concrete mixture and is therefore the opposite of segregation, where the components
of the fresh concrete are separated, and bleeding, where water is disunited from the concrete.
A concrete or mortar mixture that cannot easily be manipulated is unlikely to yield the
expected strength or durability characteristics.
Several factors inﬂuences the workability. An increase in the water content improves the con-
sistency and consequently the workability, while reducing the water content has the opposite
20
==PAGE:34==
2. Theory
eﬀect. If the cement content is reduced a mixture with a poor ﬁnish ability is produced,
while a mixture with a high cement has excellent cohesiveness, but tend to be sticky [22].
The aggregates aﬀect the workability as well. Fine sand or angular sand, produces harsh
and unworkable mixtures, requiring more water. Admixtures, such as pozzolans can either
improve or worsen the workability, like those discussed in Section 2.4.6, while water reducing
admixtures, such as plasticisers can increase the workability.
21
==PAGE:35==
2. Theory
2.5.2
Strength Development
The strength of material is deﬁned as its ability to resist stress without failing. The appear-
ance of cracks is sometimes identiﬁed as failure. However, in the case of concrete, microscopic
cracks can appear. Concrete strength is therefore the maximum stress a given sample can
withstand [22]. When concrete reaches ﬁnal setting, little to no strength has been achieved.
Upon reaching ﬁnal setting, hardening occurs, where the reaction products ﬁlls the voids in
the paste, decreasing the porosity and permeability, and increasing the strength, Figure 2.5.1
illustrates this [22].
Figure 2.5.1: Cement paste hydration products on setting time, porosity, permeability and
strength. From [22].
The ﬁgure illustrates to a degree the critical strength-porosity relationship.
Metha and
Monterio [22] states that there exists a fundamental inverse relationship between the poros-
ity and strength of solids. Although, concrete is such a complex material, that predictions
with standard strength-porosity relations are not applicable. The general strength porosity
relations should be respected, as it is a limiting factor.
As for the workability several factors inﬂuences the strength development. The w/c ra-
tio of the concrete is an important factor. An increase in w/c ratio increases the porosity,
weakening the paste matrix. Additionally in low and medium strength concrete with normal
aggregates the w/c ratio inﬂuences the strength of the interfacial zone.
Air entrainment is a factor as well, as the incorporation of air in the concrete increases
the porosity, and decreases the strength, due to inadequate compaction or air entraining
admixtures. The w/c ratio and the cement content inﬂuences the strength lost from air
entrainment [22].
Typically the inﬂuence of aggregates on the strength is ignored. This is due to the fact that
the aggregate strength is often many times stronger than the paste matrix and the interfacial
transition zone in concrete. Although this is true, except for lightweight aggregates, several
aggregate characteristics do inﬂuence the strength, such as the shape, size, surface texture,
22
==PAGE:36==
2. Theory
as well as gradation and mineralogy [22].
2.6 |
Thermogravimetric Analysis
Thermogravimetric analysis, abbreviated TGA, is an experimental technique, where the
changes in mass of a sample is measured over time, under changing temperatures. Specif-
ically designed devices designed for TGA measurements are capable of precise changes in
temperature, operating with high temperatures such as 2000°C, or freezing temperatures
like -100°C.
The results of a TGA experiment, are a stepwise TGA curve where the variations in mass
or mass percentage are plotted against the time or sample temperature. As an alternative,
it is common to use the ﬁrst derivative of the curve, showing the rate at which the mass
changes occur. More modern TGA devices are also equipped with the necessary instruments
for recording diﬀerential thermal analysis (DTA) or diﬀerential scanning calorimetry (DSC).
Both DTA and DSC work similarly, and are capable of measuring the energy changes that
occur in a sample, thus showing exothermic or endothermic events. Information concerning
the phase changes or reactions can therefore be obtained. Using these results it is possible
to understand the physical and chemical behaviour of a sample.
The design of a TGA apparatus, generally consists of an electrical furnace, a balance and
a vacuum pump. Typically the sample is placed upon the balance and sealed within the
furnace.
The atmosphere is then evacuated and replaced with a purge gas.
The gas is
continuously pumped into the furnace during measuring, purging volatiles and preventing
contamination. The gas can either function as an inert atmosphere, the most commonly
used being nitrogen, or react with the sample.
In most cases the balance contains two
spaces for samples, one for the crucible with a sample, and an empty crucible for reference.
As the temperature in the furnace changes, the density of the gas used changes as well. This
results in an alternating buoyancy, that must be corrected. If not, the mass would appear
to increase during measuring. The measurement is corrected by doing a blank experiment
with an empty crucible, resulting in a baseline.
2.6.1
Inﬂuencing Factors
Several elements can inﬂuence the outcome of a test. The book Principles and Applications
of Thermal Analysis [29], states these as:
• Sample preparation.
• Choice of crucible.
• Heating rate.
• Furnace atmosphere.
When performing measurements it is important, that the sample is representative of the
material and remain uncontaminated.
If the material being tested is inhomogeneous, a
large sample of the material must be used. The crucible used must be made of a material
23
==PAGE:37==
2. Theory
that does not react with the sample. Concerning the heating rate, it is generally best to
use a low heating rate, avoiding reaction overlap, however at times a higher rate of change
is advisable.
2.6.2
Data Interpretation
Evaluating TGA curves, involves for the most part, the determination of the individual step
height of the mass changes. For an example of the discussed steps, see Figure 2.6.1. Ef-
fects that cause mass changes are diﬀerent thermal decompositions, forming volatile reaction
products, as well as corrosion or oxidation of metals. Several eﬀects can take place one after
another, or partially overlap. When evaluating the results of TGA, it is beneﬁcial to include
the DTG or DSC curve, to better separate the diﬀerent reactions that occur. The change
in mass can thus be determined by horizontal or tangential step evaluation and separating
the steps by lines based on the DTG or DSC curves.
If the constituent causing mass changes, at the diﬀerent steps are known, the content G
can be determined from the mass loss ∆m and initial sample mass m0. If the reactions
occurring only give a partial mass loss, such as dehydration or decarbonation the original
content mass can be found with Equation (2.6.1).
G
=
∆m
m0 ·
M
n·Mgas
(2.6.1)
Where n is the moles of gas eliminated, M the molar mass of the constituent and Mgas the
molar mass of the eliminated gas.
24
==PAGE:38==
2. Theory
According to Zhihua et al. [6], in hydrating cement paste, the changes in mass in the temper-
ature range around 95°C, are caused by either C-S-H gels or ettringite. Changes appearing
around 425°C are caused by the dehydration of Porlandite.
The mass loss occurring at
around 425°C is therefore an indicator of the amount of Ca(OH)2 in the sample, and can
serve as an indicator of the degree of hydration. At around 700 °C, the mass change is likely
caused by calcite in the raw materials and therefore decarbonation. The discussed changes
in mass, are illustrated in Figure 2.6.1 as well.
Figure 2.6.1: TGA (blue) and DTG (black) curves of cement paste. From [30].
Zhihua, Baoguo and Shouwei [6], analyse cement paste at diﬀerent stages of curing, ranging
from an hour to 28 days. They noted an increase in Ca(OH)2, gradually becoming stronger
with the evolution of hydration. Lilkov et al. [7] performed a termogravimetric analysis on
cement samples after 48 days of hydration with and without coal ﬂy ash and silica fume ad-
ditives. They found that the samples without any additives contained 16.8% portlandite and
samples with additives 11.3-13.5%. By conducting a termogravimetric analysis on curing ce-
ment paste samples with diﬀerent additives, the eﬀectiveness of the additives can be deduced
by calculating the amount of portlandite at diﬀerent ages of curing, using Equation (2.6.1).
The following atomic masses are used, provided by General Chemistry [31].
• Ca: 40.8 u.
• H: 1.008 u.
• O: 16.00 u.

3
Experimental work and
materials
During the project a large amount of experimental work and research was done. The exper-
imental process was not divided into diﬀerent stages, where observations done during the
project served as incitement for further work. Rather, the entire process was goal driven, at-
tempting to discern the eﬀectiveness of wood based biomass ash and mine tailings, as cement
replacing additives, using thermogravimetric analysis. As discussed in Section 2.1, it was
clear that research analysing cement hydration with additives of wood ash and mine tailing
was lacking, as well as research utilizing thermogravimetric analysis and X-ray diﬀraction.
It was therefore decided, at the beginning, that the project should include thermogravi-
metric analysis of curing cement paste. Due to the size and scope of the project it was
decided to opt out analysis with x-ray diﬀraction. Of the two methods, processing TGA re-
sults are both less time consuming and easier to perform, making thermogravimetric analysis
the logical choice for large amounts of testing. Furthermore, it was decided to investigate the
compression strength of mortar samples with each additive, simultaneously with the cement
paste. As mentioned earlier, the pozzolanic reaction is considered slow, while the eﬀects
of wood ash and mine tailings additives are relatively unknown. For these reasons it was
determined, that to best discern the eﬀect of wood ash and mine tailings, the compressive
strength and hydration were to be analysed after 1, 3, 7, 14, 28, 60 and 90 days.
The optimal replacement ratio for the ashes and tailings is not easily distinguished, since the
replacement ratio, varies with each type of ash and tailing. The diﬀerent articles discussed
in Section 2.1, utilized diﬀerent substitution amounts, with some testing a low substitution
amount, and others replacing as much as 40% of the total binder weight. At ﬁrst, to best
test the eﬀectiveness of the ash and tailings, replacement ratios of 5, 10, 20 and 30% where
planed. However, it became evident, that a high replacement amount would tamper with
the mortar workability. Instead, the compressive strength and hydration were tested with 5
and 10% replacement at water cement ratio equal to 0.5.
To better understand the eﬀect, the substitution amount has on the workability, and there-
fore the ﬁnal strength, replacement ratios of 5, 10, 20 and 30% were tested at a much smaller
scale. It was decided to test the compressive strength at 90 days of curing for all the ratios
and compare it with the mortar ﬂow value.
27

Sand
According to DS/EN 196-1 2005, the sand used for mortar sample preparation, should be

3. Experimental work and materials
3.2.5
Amager Wood Ash
Wood ash from Amagerværket consists of burned hard and soft wood. The wood stems from
timber production, possibly containing bark, making it primary residues. The wood comes
from Baltikum and Portugal, with shipments from north America. Amagerværket burns
350.000 tones of wood a year, with about 3000 tones of ash left over. The yearly amount
burned, changes depending on demand. The wood is burned at a lower temperature than
coal, at around 1000-1100°C. The ash is cooled during transport from the ﬁlters to the silos.
The ash is used as landﬁll or is deposited in Norway [32]. Amager ash is a ﬁne grey powder,
see Figure 3.2.1a. Amager ash can be identiﬁed as AMA.
3.2.6
Køge Wood Ash
In contrast to Amagerværket, ash from Køge Kraftvarmeværk consists of leftover wood
chips and dust from ﬂoor manufacturing. If the demand for heating is high, wood chips
from forests are used as well, making it secondary residues. The chips and dust are burned
at 900-1000°C. The yearly amount of burnables is estimated at 140.00 tones, with 1100
tones of bottom and ﬂy ash leftover. The ash is cooled with water during storage. The ash
is deposited in Norway as well [32]. As a result of the water cooling the ash is lumped, with
a brown colour, see Figure 3.2.1b. Køge Ash results are indexed under KOE.
(a) Amager ash.
(b) Køge ash.

Nalunaq Mine Taillings
Nalunaq is a closed goldmine located in southern Greenland. The mine was open from 2004
to 2013, and produced 50.000-70.000 tones of tailings a year. The tailings consists primarily
of quarts, amfobolit and dolerit [33]. The mined ore was crushed, milled and treated by
cyanide leaching in an on site extraction plant. The tailings were stored in a tailing dam
located in previous parts of the mine [33]. Nalunaq tailings consists of ﬁne particles with a
light green texture, see Figure 3.2.2a. Nalunaq results are indexed under NMT.

The mine has been in use since 1857.
The type of ore body is sedi-
mentary exhalative zinc, lead, copper and silver [34]. The prcocessing of the mined ore
transpires on site, and is a combination of grinding and bulk/selective ﬂotation. The mine
produces 850.000 tones of tailings a year, consisting primarily of quarts feldspat and calcit
[33]. Tailings from Zinkgruvan consists of ﬁne particles, with a grey colour, see Figure 3.2.2b.

3. Experimental work and materials
3.3 |
Material Testing
The following sections describes how the diﬀerent materials was assessed, the samples pre-
pared and how the experimental work was performed.
3.3.1
Material Assessment
The material assessment were done with the help of the laboratory technicians at the section
for Arctic Engineering and Sustainable Solutions at DTU Byg. The tests were done, as spec-
iﬁed in the section guides [35]. This does not include the X-ray diﬀraction and ﬂuorescence,
or the thermogravimetric analysis. The thermogravimetric analysis is described in depth in
a later section of the chapter. The results of each subsequent test done on the materials use,
are included in Appendix B.
Particle Size Distribution
The particle size distribution, and thereby the ﬁneness, of the six additives and cement
used, were measured by laser diﬀraction, using a Mastersizer 2000 laser diﬀractor. Three
measurements were made for each of the additives as well as the cement. The averaged
results are included as part of Section B.9.
pH Value and Conductivity
It is important to know the potential chemical properties of the additives, and how they
would work in concrete. For this purpose the pH value and conductivity are determined.
Measurements were done with triplicate determination and the results can be viewed in
Section B.5.
Volumetric Content of Carbonate
As the wood ash and mine tailings potentially contain a sizeable amount of calcium carbonate
that might aﬀect the cement hydration, as earlier speciﬁed, knowing the amount is crucial.
The carbonate content is measured with triplicate determination as well.
Measurement
results are located in Section B.6.
Loss on Ignition
Loss on ignition expresses the amount of burnable matter in the additives, such as organic
mater in the wood ashes. The loss on ignition is measured at 550°C and 950°C. The mass
loss can therefore also include decarbonation and dehydration The loss on ignition is used
in conjunction with the thermogravimetric analysis. The LOI is evaluated with triplicate
measurement. Results are displayed in Section B.3 and Section B.4.
Water Content and Solubility
As part of the assessment, the water content in the additives is measured. The water does not
pose a problem when preparing samples, as the additives are dried beforehand. However, it
32
==PAGE:46==
3. Experimental work and materials
is important in understanding the materials in question. The solubility is likewise important.
As a measure of the matter dissolving during mortar preparation, it can provide insight in
how much reactive material is present. The water content and solubility is measured with
triplicate determination. Section B.1 and Section B.2 contains the analysis results.
Water Soluble Anions and Cations
The concentration of water soluble anions in the six admixtures is found with ion chro-
matography (IC). The anions determined with this method are Cl−, NO−
3 and SO2−
4 . The
cations are determined with inductively Coupled Plasma Optical Emission Spectrometry
(ICP-OES). The cations determined are Na+, K+, Mg2+ and Ca2+. The results are in-
cluded in Section B.7. This is done for the coal ﬂy ash and silica fume.
Chemical Content of Elements
The chemical content of elements in the diﬀerent additives are measured in two ways. The
ﬁrst is with ICP-OES, the second is x-ray ﬂorescence. The x-ray ﬂorescence was done by
Force TECHNOLOGY. The results determined by them, are included in Section B.10. The
ICP-OES results are included in Section B.8. X-ray ﬂorescence is precise when measuring a
large content of elements, while the ICP-OES works well for measuring elements present in
small amounts.
X-ray Diﬀraction
To identify the diﬀerent mineral phases present in the additives and cement, a PanAlytical
X-ray diﬀractometer is utilized. The diﬀractometer has 3 characteristic instrument stages,
where the PW3064 Spinner stage for standard powder samples has been used. To ensure a
proper analysis, the grain size of the samples, should be less than 25 µm at best, between
5-10 µm. Samples to be tested are therefore crushed beforehand, and carefully compressed
into sample holders, avoiding contamination and undesired packing. The necessary data are
then measured using the program X’Pert Data Collector. Data treatment is done using the
program X’Pert HighScore Plus, with data from the international Centre for Diﬀraction Data
(ICDD). Basic phase identiﬁcation is used for identifying the compounds in the samples.
33
==PAGE:47==
3. Experimental work and materials
3.3.2
Sample Preparation
The proportions by mass of each batch of mortar prepared consists of 450 ± 2 g of cement,
1350 ± 5 g of sand and 225 ± 1 g of water. The 450 g cement, include any added admixture.
The diﬀerent recipes used for each percentage of admixture replacement, are included with
Table 3.3.1. The diﬀerent cement pastes, are made using the same proportions, excluding
the sand.
Tabel 3.3.1: Cement replacement admix.

The mortar and cement paste are mixed using a 5 liter N-50 Horbart table mixer, see
Figure 3.3.1. The stainless steel bowl and steel blade of the mixer is in accordance with
DS/EN 196-1:2005. The mixer has three gears, where the ﬁrst and second gear complies
with the low and high speed of the mixer blade, as described in the standard.
Figure 3.3.1: N-50 Horbart table mixer.
34
==PAGE:48==
3. Experimental work and materials
Mortar Preparation
The mortar batches used for compression testing and ﬂow tests are prepared in the following
manner. The required cement and admix, according to the recipe used, are weighed and
added to the stainless steel bowl. The sand and water are weighed in separate containers.
The mortar is then mixed using the following procedure.
• At zero time, water is added and the mixer runs at low speed.
• After 30 s of mixing, the sand is steadily added over the next 30 s.
• At 1 minute, the mixer is switched to high speed and mixed for an additional 30 s.
• After 90 s, the mixer is stopped for 90 s.
• During the ﬁrst 30 s of the stop, the bowl sides are scraped.
• At the end of the 90 s pause, the mixing is continued at high speed for 60 s.
• After a total of 4 minutes the mixing is done.
At this stage the mortar is either used directly for ﬂow testing, see Section 3.3.4, or cast for
later compressive testing.
The casting is done with a mould consisting of three 40x40x160 mm3 compartments, meet-
ing the demands of DS/EN 196-1:2005, see Figure 3.3.2a, for a total of 3 prism specimen
see Figure 3.3.2b. The moulds are placed in advance on a vibrating table, and ﬁlled halfway
with the mixed mortar. The mould is vibrated for 30 s at 55-60 Hz, ﬁlled completely and
vibrated for another 30 s.
Any excess mortar is removed from the mould with a metal
straigthedge, smoothing the surface. The ﬁlled mould is then covered with plastic and left
to set for 20-24 hours. After the initial setting time the specimens are de-moulded and
submerged in de-mineralized water and left to harden, until testing. Specimens to be tested
immediately after 24 hours, are de-moulded 20 minutes before testing.
(a) Three compartment mould.
(b) Mortar prisms.
Figure 3.3.2: Mould and prisms.
35
==PAGE:49==
3. Experimental work and materials
Cement Paste Preparation
The cement pastes made for thermogravimetric analysis are mixed in the same manner, as
the batches of mortar, with the exception of excluding the step containing sand. The dif-
ferent proportions of ingredients for each recipe used for the mortar, are used for the paste
as well. Instead of casting the cement paste as prisms, the paste is poured into cylinders
with a diameter of 14 mm, see Figure 3.3.3a. After the 4 minutes of mixing, the paste is
transferred to a plastic bag. The bag is then sealed, and a small hole is cut, making it easier
to pour the paste into the cylinders. If needed, the paste is vibrated at 50 Hz. Each batch
ﬁlls 8 to 10 cylinders.
To prevent bleeding, the cylinders are placed on a centrifuge for the ﬁrst 20-24 hours,
see Figure 3.3.3b, afterwards the pastes are removed from the cylinders, and left to cure in
containers with distilled water.
After curing for the required amount of time, the paste samples are crushed, ﬁrst with
a jaw crusher then a hammer, and ultimately ground into a ﬁne powder using a pestle and
mortar. In order to ensure no further hydration occurs, the powder is placed in a vacuum
desiccator for more than an hour, evaporating any unbound water in the powder.
The
prepared paste powder is then stored in sealed plastic bags until testing.
(a) Cylinders used for initial curing.
(b) Centrifuge used during initial curing.
Figure 3.3.3: Caption of animals
36
==PAGE:50==
3. Experimental work and materials
3.3.3
Compressive Strength Testing
The compressive strength of the prepared specimens are tested using a 200 ton universal
testing machine from M&F AG, see Figure 3.3.4. The machine is of older making, running
on hydraulic oil pressure, pushing a ram along its vertical axis. It is manually operated
and ﬁtted with a pacing device that controls the oil pressure, and therefore the rate of load
increase. The original pressure indicator has been exchanged for a digital read out capable of
recording the maximum value achieved. Additionally it is ﬁtted with two load ranges 20 kN
and 200 kN. Due to its manual operating, the machine does not speciﬁcally provide a setting
for a rate of load increase of 2.4 ± 0.2 kN
s , as otherwise speciﬁed in DS/EN 196-1.
Figure 3.3.4: M&F 200 ton universal testing machine.
37
==PAGE:51==
3. Experimental work and materials
The machine is equipped with a ﬂexure device, complying with DS/EN 196-1, for ﬂexural
strength testing, see Figure 3.3.5a, and a jig for compression testing, consisting of two 2 cm
thick and 4 cm wide steel blocks, see Figure 3.3.5b. This type of machine is chosen for the
compressive testing, due to two reasons. It is relatively easy to operate, and the sample
prisms ﬁt well in the machine, requiring no additional components for the compression
tests.
(a) Flexural strength testing.
(b) Compression strength.
Figure 3.3.5: Flexure and compression device.
Before testing, each of the prepared samples height are measured, in order to calculate the
compression area. The individual prisms are then halved, using the ﬂexural part of the
machine. The ﬂexural strength measured is noted, but is not otherwise recorded as part of
the project. Each of the halves are then compression tested, thus each specimen is tested
twice, resulting in a total of 6 compression tests for each batch of mortar cast. The average
of the 6 tests are then chosen as the compressive strength. At ﬁrst, the rate of load increase
is high, while the specimens initially hits the top steel block. Afterwards, the load increase
is adjusted to 0.1-0.2 kN
s
and as much as possible, kept constant until compression failure.
A low rate of load increase is chosen to better determine the fracture load at failure. The
strength tests at the diﬀerent ages are carried out within the limits described in DS/EN
196-1:2005.
According to DS/EN 196-1 2005, the average of the tests ± 10% should be determined.
If one of the results obtained fall outside this margin, it should be discarded, and a new
average based on the remaining found. If another result falls outside the new ± 10% average,
the batch should be discarded and tested anew. In the majority of cases this term is upheld.
However, in the few cases where it is not, the batch is not discarded. See Appendix C for
the results.
38
==PAGE:52==
3. Experimental work and materials
3.3.4
Mortar Workability
The workability of the diﬀerent mortar batches are determined, based on the ﬂow value
found with DS/EN 1015-3:1999. The test measures the consistency of the mortar. The
standard refers to mortar for masonry. However, the same procedures are relevant for the
mortar used as part of this project. The consistency is measured using a ﬂow table with a
tamper, as speciﬁed by DS/EN 1015-3:1999, see Figure 3.3.6. According to DS/EN 1015-
3:1999, a mould with a height of 60 mm ± 0.5 mm, internal diameter at the top of 70 mm
± 0.5 mm and an internal diameter at the bottom of 100 mm ± 0.5 mm should be used.
A mould with the speciﬁc dimensions was unavailable, instead a mould with a height of 50
mm were used.
The testing procedure, is as follows.
• The mould is placed centrally on the table and ﬁlled halfway.
• The mortar is compacted with 10 short strokes of the tamper.
• The mould is ﬁlled completely and compacted with another 10 strokes.
• After a 15 s pause, the mould is slowly removed.
• The mortar is then spread, by jolting the table 15 times with a jolt per second.
During each test the ﬂow value is measured twice, one measurement across each axis. The
ﬂow value of the mortar is calculated as the mean value of two measurements.
Figure 3.3.6: Flow table for measuring the consistency.
39
==PAGE:53==
3. Experimental work and materials
3.3.5
TGA Testing
The thermogravimetric analysis of the diﬀerent cement paste samples, are done using the
simultaneous thermal analyser NETZSCH STA 449 F3 Jupiter, see Figure 3.3.7a. Depend-
ing on the furnace used, the machine is able to operate within the temperature ranges of
-150°C and 2400°C, with heating and cooling rates of 0.001
K
min to 50
K
min.
The model
used were ﬁtted with a silicon carbide furnace. The machine utilizes top loading, and has
several diﬀerent parts and modes of operation. All of the measurements were done as a joint
TGA and DSC measurement, the set-up for TGA-DSC were therefore used, together with
aluminium oxide forms, see Figure 3.3.7b.
The forms are ﬁlled with the prepared samples. If needed the sample is crushed further.
The weight of each sample varies. However, around 50 mg have been measured for every test.
A three step temperature program is used when measuring. The steps are:
• The temperature is raised to 29 °C.
• For 10 minutes the temperature is held constant at 29 °C.
• The temperature is then increased to 1000 °C, at 10
K
min.
When 1000°C is reached, the measurement is ﬁnished. The oven is then left to cool down
to room temperature, without an added cooling step. As a precaution, if the temperature
were to reach 1010°C the measurement would terminate. As a purge gas, a constant ﬂow
rate of nitrogen is used. When the measurement has ﬁnished the data is loaded with the
analysis program "Protheus analyzer", and subsequently processed. As a default the
TGA and DSC is measured against time. This is changed to temperature.
(a) NETZSCH STA 449 F3 Jupiter.
(b) Aluminium oxide forms.
Figure 3.3.7: Thermogravimetric analysis apparatus.
40

3. Experimental work and materials

4
Results and Discussion
In the following sections, the results of the experimental process are discussed and concluded
upon. It is divided into several sections. First they focus on the material assessment, then
on the results of the sample testing.
4.1 |
Material assessment
The ﬁrst section concerns the results of the material assessment.
4.1.1
Characteristics
The characterisation results of the six additives are included in Table 4.1.1. The results
indicate that wood ash from Amagerværket has the largest pH of the six additives used. It
can be noted that the two wood based ashes have the highest pH of the additives. Further-
more the analysis shows that the ashes as a whole achieves a higher pH, than mine tailings
and silica fume does. The high pH of the wood ashes was however to be expected, as this
complies with the literature [18]. Wherein wood ash, when mixed with water, achieves a
high pH. Additionally, the ashes has the highest conductivity and water solubility as well,
with the exception of the coal ﬂy ash, where the silica fume is more soluble. Adverse to
the ashes, the mine tailings does not appear to have a high pH, conductivity or solubility,
likewise for the silica fume.
Tabel 4.1.1: Additives characterisation.
