As stated in Concrete [22], a well hydrated Portland cement paste consisting of relatively
insoluble hydrates of calcium exists in a state of stable equilibrium with a high pH pore ﬂuid.
The pH value ranges from 12.5 to 13.5, depending on the amount of Na+ K+ and OH−
ions in the ﬂuid. Furthermore, a reduction of the pore ﬂuids alkalinity, would eventually
43
==PAGE:57==
4. Results and Discussion
lead to a destabilization of the products of hydration. The ashes, predominantly the wood
based ashes, would appear to contribute to the alkalinity of the cement paste. Addition-
ally, a low pH, would sabotage the passivity of the environment surrounding any potential
reinforcement, starting the corrosion process. The mine tailings, does not appear to have a
positive contribution to the alkaline environment. However, [22] states that a low concrete
permeability and an aggressive ﬂuid with a pH above 6, does not threaten the concrete
signiﬁcantly in the case of chemical attacks.
The high conductivity and solubility of the wood based ashes, was excepted, as this com-
plies with the theory, stating that wood ash contains salt with a high solubility [18]. The
conductivity gives an indication of the amount of soluble ions in the ashes, and therefore the
potential reactivity of the ashes. The mine tailings, appear to posses little soluble material
and a low conductivity. The amount of soluble ions in the tailings are therefore small, with
an expected low reactivity.
The physically bound water of the 6 additives varies greatly.
Amager ash, coal ﬂy ash
and silica fume all have less than 1 wt% water, making the water content negligible. The
high amount of water in Køge ash, is likely a result of the production method. As stated
in chapter 3, water is added to cool the ash during depositing, resulting in a high water
content. The amount of water in the mine tailings is either from process water, weather, or
a combination of both.
The calcium carbonate test, showed that, the wood based ashes contains the highest amount,
with Køge ash consisting of 33.52 %. According to the discussed theory, calcium was the
major constituent in wood ash, as well as the likely formation of carbonates, a high amount
of calcium carbonate is to be expected [18]. The amount of calcium carbonate in the coal
ﬂy ash and silica fume, are negligible, or otherwise of a magnitude, so that any carbonate
measured could be considered an error. Both additives are unlikely to contain carbonate.
Compared to the wood ash, the carbonate content in the tailings are low, with the amount
in Zinkgruvan tailings being almost 4 times greater than in Nalunaq tailings. According to
Jensen et al. [33], the Zinkgruvan tailings contain calcite. However, the amount measured
seem to low to call it a major component.
The loss on ignition obtained at 550°C, gives an indication of the amount of organic matter
or other volatile matter in the additives. From the table it can be seen, that the amount of
organic matter in the Amager ash is low. For the coal ﬂy ash and Køge ash, it is above 2 and
5% respectively. Thus, the ash from Køge Kraftvarmeværk contains a signiﬁcant amount
of organic matter, unwanted when casting concrete. The higher amount of organic matter
in the Køge ash, is likely a result of the lower furnace temperature used. The tailings are
shown to contain little volatile matter, up to 550°C. The silica fume does not appear to
contain much volatile matter.
At 950°C dehydration and decarbonation can have transpired. The mass loss of the wood
ashes increases tremendously, likely due to the high amount of calcium carbonate measured.
44
==PAGE:58==
4. Results and Discussion
The LOI of the tailings increases as well. This can be attributed to the calcium carbonate
as well, as the increase is highest for Zinkgruvan. The LOI of coal ﬂy ash and sillica fume
is not increased in any signiﬁcant way.
4.1.2
Thermogravimetric Analysis
Figure 4.1.1 and Figure 4.1.2 depicts the TGA patterns of the 6 additives and the basis ce-
ment used. Included in Section E.1 are the TGA and DSC curves of each of the 6 additives
individually.
The residual mass of the coal ﬂy ash and silica fume is 1.07 and 0.75% respectively. The loss
on ignition measured, does not correspond to the residual mass of the TGA. The correspond-
ing DSC curves does not show any peaks, implying that no decarbonation and dehydration
takes place or other reactions occur. The low mass loss is therefore understandable. The
diﬀerences in mass between the two methods is likely a result of the burning method and
atmosphere of two types of oven used. During the LOI measurement, the samples are left at
a high temperature for several hours, while with TGA the samples are heated to just below
1000°C where the measurement immediately ends, implying that further loss of mass could
be possible.
The TGA pattern for basis cement matches that of Amager ash and Køge ash. Around
700°C a large mass loss occurs, with little mass loss up until this point. The cement and
the two ashes, all contain CaCO3, that undergoes decarbonation at around 700°C . The
two ashes both contain more carbonate than the cement, explaining the larger drop. Of
the two, Køge ash contains the most. Køge ash loses more mass before 700°C , due to a
higher content of unburned organic matter. Additionally, at approx. 400°C the DSC curve
peaks, due the presence of Ca(OH)2 undergoing dehydration. It is not present in Amager
ash. The DSC curves of the two ashes, possess two distinct peaks at around 700°C . One
is attributed to the presence of CaCO3, the second is unknown, indicating the presence of
another compound that reacts at such high temperatures. The residual mass lost is greater
with TGA than the LOI measurement, almost doubled in the case of Køge ash. The reasons
for this is unknown. The article by Bennack et al [14], utilizes thermogravimetric analysis
during material characterisation. They attributed the mass loss to carbonate decomposition
as well, but also sulfate dehydration and the transformation of quartz from one state to an-
other. The XRF analysis, see Section 4.1.3 showed that the Si content of the ashes was low,
making quartz transformation unlikely. Additionally, the assessment, done on the ashes,
revealed no sulphate phases capable of dehydration. Instead the peaks could potentially
belong to the potassium salts found present in the ashes.
45
==PAGE:59==
4. Results and Discussion
Figure 4.1.1: TGA of basis cement, coal ﬂy ash, Amager ash and Køge ash.
Figure 4.1.2: TGA of silica fume, Nalunaq and Zinkgruvan tailings.
46
==PAGE:60==
4. Results and Discussion
The mass loss of Nalunaq and Zinkgruvan is similar to that of the LOI measurement, albeit
slightly larger as well.
The majority of the mass loss occur after 700°C.
This can be
attributed to the calcium carbonate content, that was shown to be higher in Zinkgruvan.
The DSC curve of Nalunaq is rather plain in comparison to the wood ashes and Zinkgruvan.
Further indicative of the low amount of calcite.
4.1.3
Chemical Content of Elements
Table 4.1.2 contains the chemical content of elements measured with x-ray ﬂorescence. All of
the six additives analysed contain silicon, albeit in varied content. Silica fume contains the
largest amount of silicon, as can be expected of a powder consisting of 85-90% amorphous
silica. It, however, demonstrates the amount of silicon needed to achieve a high pozzolanic-
ity. Interestingly, the content of the other elements in silica fume is under 1% with the
exception of sodium. Table 4.1.3 contains the elemental content measured with ICP. The
ICP is seemingly more eﬀective when measuring small quantities. The remaining element
content is here measured as below 0.1%.
Coal ﬂy ash appear to contain half the amount of silicon, than silica fume contains. The other
major components of the ﬂy ash are aluminium and iron, with small amounts of calcium
and potassium. The elemental content measured is as expected. The major components of
a pozzolanic coal ﬂy ash is silicon, aluminium and iron. The small amount of calcium could
indicate that the coal ﬂy ash used is a low calcium ﬂy ash. With smaller proportions of
silicon, coal ﬂy ash is expected to be less pozzolanic than silica fume.
The two types of tailing contain high amounts of silicon, although less than coal ﬂy ash.
The silicon is likely contained in silicates as described in the theory section. The second
largest constituent in the tailings is calcium, likely in the form of the calcium carbonate,
previously discussed. Although both tables show, that Nalunaq contains the highest amount
of Ca, indicating the presence of other calcium containing phases. The two tailings contains
various amounts of potassium, aluminium, iron and magnesium. Neither of the 4 metals are
mined for at Zinkgruvan and Nalunaq, and are therefore likely present in the mined ore, but
otherwise not extracted during the second stage of processing. Table 4.1.3 indicates small
amounts of lead and zink in the tailings from Zinkgruvan, indicating the presence of the
economic product in small amounts. Compared with the tailings used in articles [15],[16]
and [8] diﬀerences and similarities can be seen. The iron tailings used by Yunhong et al [8]
contained, like Nalunaq and Zinkgruvan, Si, Fe and Al as its major components, with small
amounts of Mg. Compared to the tailings used in articles [15] and [16], the Zinkgruvan
and Nalunaq tailings appear more varied, as the tailings of the articles contained almost
exclusively Fe and Si respectively.
47

4. Results and Discussion
The silicon content of Amager ash and Køge ash is 0.70 and 2.10% respectively. The silicon
content of the wood ashes is signiﬁcantly smaller than the coal ash, and comparable to the
fume and tailings. Instead, the two ashes contain high amounts of calcium and potassium.
Additionally, of the two, Amager ash has the highest content of calcium and potassium. As
the previous analysis showed, the calcium is likely present in the form of calcium carbonate.
However, the carbonate content was found to be higher in Køge ash, that has a low calcium
count. The calcium determined is likely also present as another chemical compound. The
higher sodium content in Køge ash, is in line with the higher sodium ion content measured
see Section 4.1.4. Compared to the mean measurements of Dominik et al. [18], the two
ashes contain more Ca and K, but less Al and Fe. Compared to the measurements made
in the articles shown in Section 2.1, Amager ash and Køge ash contain a signiﬁcantly higher
amount of calcium. The ashes of the articles contain a signiﬁcant portion of silicate instead.
In fact the ash described as high calcium ash by Cheah et al [10], contains less calcium than
Amager and Køge ash. The eﬀectiveness of Amager ash and Køge ash is therefore presumed
limited.
49
==PAGE:63==
4. Results and Discussion
4.1.4
Water Soluble Anions and Cations
The results of the anions and cations measurement are included in Table 4.1.4. From the
table it can be seen that of the 6 additives, it is both Amager ash and Køge ash that contain
water soluble ions in large amounts. The measured content of ions in the tailings, coal ﬂy
ash and silica is less than 0.05 % for all the diﬀerent ions. The only ion measured in any
substantial amount is sulphate, it being highest in the Zinkgruvan tailings and coal ﬂy ash.
This matches the measured conductivity and water solubility, where coal ﬂy ash was shown
to have the higher conductivity and solubility. Additionally, of the two tailings, Zinkgruvan
had the highest conductivity and solubility. This does not explain why the conductivity and
solubility of the silica fume was as high as measured. Thus, the addition of tailings, coal ﬂy
ash or silica fume does not appear to aﬀect the pore solution.
As the measured conductivity and solubility was highest for the wood ashes, vast quantities
of soluble ions have been measured. Of the measured ions it is clear that chloride, sulphate,
sodium and potassium are at the forefront. It is further shown, that Amager ash mainly
consists of sulphate and potassium ions, while Køge ash consists of chloride and sodium as
well. This conforms with the chemical content analysis. It showed Køge ash as having a
higher Cl and Na amount, while Amager ash contained more K. The high solubility of the
wood ashes is likely due to potassium salts, as Dominik et al [18] suggests.

4. Results and Discussion
4.1.5
XRD Analysis
Figure 4.1.3 shows the x-ray diﬀraction patterns of the wood ashes and tailings. The diﬀrac-
tograms conﬁrms the presence of calcium carbonate in the ashes. Although measured as
present in small amounts in the tailings, it was not further conﬁrmed present using XRD.
Given that the major element in the two wood ashes are Ca, and the results of volumetric
carbonate measurement, the dominant phase in the ashes appears to be CaCO3. However,
the analysis also conﬁrms the presence of other calcium connections as well. Amager ash
potentially contains CaO. This is a likely scenario, as the resulting CaCO3 could be the
result of CaO carbonation during cooling and storage. CaO was not determined as part
of Køge ash. Instead peaks for Ca(OH)2 was found. The Ca(OH)2 peaks are likely the
result of the water cooling procedure by the CHP plant. Upon contact with water, CaO
reacts and forms Ca(OH)2. This is certainly an unwanted trait for the ashes. If mixed
with cement and water, part of the water would instead react with the CaO, resulting in
additional Ca(OH)2 formation and potentially expansion [24]. This is not expected to be a
problem as the CaO content of each ash is suspected to be low. The high amount of calcium
carbonate in the ashes could potentially function as an accelerator for the cement [23]. The
eﬀect of this acceleration is unknown, as the basis cement used contain limestone ﬁller.
The XRD analysis conﬁrms the presence of silicon oxide in both ashes and the tailings. This
could infer the potential of pozzolanic activity. However, XRD determines the crystalline
minerals present and therefore not the presence of amorphous silicon dioxide. According to
Metha et al. [22], crystalline minerals such as quartz are non-reactive at ordinary tempera-
tures. The silicon dioxide found using x-ray diﬀraction would therefore have low pozzolanic
potential. Amorphous SiO2 may be present in the wood ashes in small amounts. SEM anal-
ysis done as part of a previous project investigating Amager ash and Køge ash determined
the presence of spherical glassy particles similar to that of coal ﬂy ash [32]. Based on the
tailings origins, the presence of amorphous silica is not expected.
Other than CaCo3 and SiO2, Amager ash shows peaks for MgO and K2SO4. As stated in
Section 2.4.3, MgO can react with water forming Mg(OH)2 similar to CaO. Other than
using the available water, when mixed with cement as well, the reaction is expansive and can
lead to crack formation. The chemical content analysis showed a low content of magnesium,
it is therefore judged as unproblematic. Potassium sulphate is likely present in the ash, as
the potassium content was found to be high in Amager ash. Additionally the major anions
in the ash was found to be sulphate and potassium. According to Kurdowski [23], potassium
sulphate is a strong accelerator of hardening. Coupled with the high amount of CaCO3,
Amager ash could potentially function as an accelerator. Peaks belonging to either MgO or
K2SO4 was not found in Køge ash. Instead KCl peaks was determined. This is not unlikely,
as Køge ash was found to possess less potassium and more chloride ions than Amager ash.
Although mentioned, as less of an accelerator, potassium and chloride still accelerates the
hydration of cement [23].
51
==PAGE:65==
4. Results and Discussion

P-Portlandite, S-Sylvite, Mi-Microcline, Pl-Plagioclase, X1-Iron Magnesium Aluminium Sil-

52
==PAGE:66==
4. Results and Discussion
Although it was not determined by XRD, it is likely that both Amager ash and Køge ash
contains MgO, KCl and K2SO4 in varied amounts, based on the elemental content and
ions measurement. The potential eﬀect of the ashes as accelerators, is highly speculative.
The Previous project done, working with the same two ashes [32], showed that the addition
of Køge ashes accelerated the initial setting, as well as ﬁnal setting time, while Amager ash
both retarded and accelerated the setting times. Articles such as [12] found that the initial
and ﬁnal setting time increases with the addition of wood ash as well. Other studies utilizing
wood ash, determined that their ashes was primarily quartz [14] or contained diﬀerent
minerals such as akermanite and pectolite [10]. Trace amounts of gypsum and mullite was
also determined [36]. Gypsum contained in the ashes would likely react with the aluminate,
increasing the setting times.
Other than silicon oxide, the two types of tailings shows peaks for the feldspars microcline
and plagioclase, as well as the unnamed compunds calcium aluminium silicate hydroxide,
iron magnesium aluminium silicate hydroxide and calcium magnesium iron silicate. The
eﬀect of these minerals on cement and concrete, is unknown. They do not appear to be
accelerators or retarders of hardening, neither do they appear to be pozzolanic materials.
It further shows that the silicate in the two tailings are not limited to quartz. It can be
hypothesised that, the addition of these minerals functions in the same manner as sand and
crystalline sillica. The diﬀerent minerals determinend with XRD, are likely present in the
mined ore, and left over after processing. A previous study of the two tailings [37] found
that Zinkgruvan contained dolomite, amphibole and muscovit as well. Nalunaq was found
to contain amphibole as well. According to the article by Jensen et al. [33], both feldspars
are found present in Zinkgruvan, and that Nalunaq contains plagioclase. The two studies
did not conﬁrm the presence of the unnamed compounds found. This could indicate that
they in fact are not present in the two tailings.
Figure 4.1.4 depicts diﬀractograms of basis cement, coal ﬂy ash and silica fume. The results
appear as can be expected. The basis cement shows peaks for alite, belite and aluminate.
These are the three major constituents of cement, and are most likely to be determined with
XRD. The silica fume diﬀractogram barely has any peaks, and consist for the most part of
undeterminable background material. This is likely due to the high amount of amorphous
silica in silica fume. Running a peak analysis, does indicate the presence of crystalline silica,
this is expected to be present in small amounts. The coal ﬂy ash diﬀractogram shows peaks
for silicon oxide and mulite. This was expected based on the information provided by Metha
et al. [22]. Based on the information, the coal ﬂy ash used are a low calcium ﬂy ash.

4. Results and Discussion
4.1.6
Particle Size Distribution
The particle size distributions determined for the 6 additives, as well as for the basis cement
and sand used, are included with Figure 4.1.5 and Figure 4.1.6. A clear variation in the
distributions are apparent, both between the diﬀerent types of additives and within each
type.
According to Metha et al.
[22], silica fume is two orders of magnitude ﬁner than coal
ﬂy ash and cement, with an average diameter of 0.1 µm. The distribution determined with
laser diﬀractor is signiﬁcantly larger, than the coal ﬂy ash and basis cement. The deviation
is likely the result of agglomeration. During the use of the silica fume, large lumps was
observed, that were easily breakable. It is therefore assumed that the average individual
particle size is 0.1 µm. The average particle size of cement was deﬁned as between 10-15 µm.
The basis cement used as part of this project appear slightly larger. As for the silica fume,
the cement appeared to lump as well, although to a lesser degree. According to Siddique et
al. [38], due to its ﬁneness silica fume works as a ﬁller.
From Figure 4.1.5, it is evident, that Amager ash and coal ﬂy ash, have a similar parti-
cle distribution, mirroring each other. Both curves develop similarly. However, it appears
that Amager ash consists of marginally larger particles than coal ﬂy ash. The particle size
of the ashes, seem to lie within the size range of the cement. In comparison, Køge ash shows
a diﬀerent cumulative size distribution. The particle size range is greater, with particles
between 1 and 10 µm and as large as the largest sand particles. This extreme range, likely
stems from the unburned organic matter in the ash and the water cooling. Upon cooling,
the ash forms larger agglomerates, changing the characteristics. It would appear, that for
both the coal ﬂy ash and Amager ash 90% of the particles are less than 100 µm. Moosber-
Bustnes[28] deﬁned ﬁllers as particles with a maximum size of 125 µm. Based on this criteria,
the coal ﬂy ash and Amager ash appear to have potential ﬁller capabilities. However, Køge
ash appears to consist of less than 40% particles smaller than 100 µm, making it unsuitable
as a ﬁller.
Of the two tailings, Nalunaq has the smallest size distribution, with particles smaller than
10 µm and larger than 100 µm. Although the Nalunaq distribution is larger than Amager
ash and coal ﬂy ash, it does have particles the same size as the cement. The overall particle
size distribution however, is closer to that of the sand used. Zinkgruvan tailings are coarser,
with fewer particles below 10 µm. Although larger, Zinkgruvan and Nalunaq tailings follow
a similar pattern. Zinkgruvan tailings closely resembles the distribution of sand. It is quite
clear that Zinkgruvan particles are to large to function as ﬁllers. Nalunaq appear to consist
of 60% particles with a size smaller than 100 µm. Thus it could have some potential as a
ﬁller, however this would appear to be limited. The same article [28], discussed the possibil-
ity of otherwise inert quartz, to have pozzolanic capabilities if the particle size is less than 5
µm. It would, however, seem that the tailings are to large to uphold this criteria. Amager
ash does seem to have particles less than 5 microns, but it was, however, shown to possess
little silicate.

4. Results and Discussion
4.2 |
Compressive Strength: Curing Time
The following sections concerns the compressive strength measured for the diﬀerent mortar
samples, at diﬀerent points of curing. When depicting the strength as a function of curing
time, with several measurements and additives, the mean was shown to not always ﬁt per-
fectly with the rest of the curve. This shows itself in the following graphs as dips in strength
at certain points and relatively ﬂat areas. Such diﬀerences in strength are potentially due
to diﬀerences in the hydration schemes or possible the workability of the diﬀerent mortar
samples. The workability is discussed further in Section 4.4.
4.2.1
5% Substitution
The compressive strengths measured over time are depicted in Figure 4.2.1 for mortar sam-
ples with 5% substitution. The normalised compressive strengths in terms of the reference
samples are displayed in Figure 4.2.2.
From the ﬁgures the eﬀectiveness of adding coal ﬂy ash and silica fume as well as the
diﬀerences between the two types of pozzolans are apparent. The ﬁgures demonstrate how
the pozzolanic reaction under normal circumstances is considered slow, while in the case of
silica fume addition, develops exceptionally fast. With 5% silica fume replacement, the same
strength as the reference is achieved before 7 days of curing. In terms of the normalised
plot, this is seen as an almost constant strength between 1 and 3 days of curing, at approx.
93% of the reference, followed by a steep increase in strength. In comparison the coal ﬂy as
achieves 100% reference strength around 48 days of hydration.
The early age strength increase from silica fume substitution is quite signiﬁcant. In the
period 7-28 days, the strength keeps increasing from day to day. This is especially easy to
see with Figure 4.2.2, where after 28 days of curing, the compressive strength is equal to
approx. 115% of the reference. However, more important, the slope of the normalised plot
between 7, 14 and 28 days of curing decreases. After 28 days of hydration the strength
increase subsides, while the strength of the reference samples still increases. In terms of
Figure 4.2.1 this is seen as little to no increase in strength between 28 an 90 days. In Fig-
ure 4.2.2 it is seen as changing negative and positive slope. Between 60 and 90 days, the
normalised strength is close to constant.
At 5% substitution, the coal ﬂy ash samples does not achieve a signiﬁcantly higher strength
than the reference. At 60 days of curing, it is seen to increase slightly beyond the reference
and then decrease below it at 90 days. This is understood as the previously mentioned
measurement uncertainties. However, it can also be interpreted, as the 5% replacements
inability to further increase the strength. Compared to silica fume, coal ﬂy ash has a longer
period of seemingly constant normalised compression strength. Between 1 and 14 days of
hydration the normalised strength ﬂuctuates between 90 and 95%. After 14 days the slope
is increased. However it decreases signiﬁcantly between 28 and 60 days.

4. Results and Discussion
Counter to silica fume and coal ﬂy ash, the mortar samples with wood ash or tailings does
not show any signs of improvement during the curing process. Interestingly, the 4 additives
appear to have similar compressive strength curves, achieving near identical strengths, albeit
with several of the aforementioned dips in strength. It is perplexing as to how, given that
the 4 additives are rather diﬀerent from one another. The normalised compressive strength
curves, do not infer any positive eﬀect of the wood ashes or tailings either. Compared to the
silica fume and coal ﬂy ash, it would appear that the wood ashes and tailing samples never
pass the constant stage. In fact, the wood ash and tailing sample strength measurements
ﬂuctuate between 85 and 95%. Although this is the case, it can be further seen that the
tailings samples after 1 day of curing achieve the lowest normalised strength at approx. 85%
but after 90 days achieve a strength approx. equal to 95%. Comparatively, the wood ash
samples appear to begin at and end at approx. 95%. The diﬀerences in early age strength
is discussed further at a later point in the thesis.
Figure 4.2.3 depicts a bar plot of the measured compressive strength, for the reference
and 5% substitution samples. The diﬀerences in the 90 day strength depicted here are much
clearer. As with the other plots, the eﬀectiveness of silica fume is easily discernible. Both
the measured strength and the standard deviation is higher than that of the reference. The
mean value of the coal ﬂy ash samples was shown to be lower than the reference. However, it
is quite clear, based on the measurement spread, that at 90 days of curing, samples with 5%
CFA replacement are capable of reaching the same strength as the reference. The samples
with wood ash and tailings all achieved a lower strength than the reference. Although, the
standard deviation of the Amager ash and Nalunaq samples are close to that of the refer-
ence, implying the ability to almost achieve the same strength as the reference. In fact, the
standard deviation of the Zinkgruvan samples infers the capability, that a 5% substitution
can reach the same compressive strength as the reference. Furthermore, it is shown, that
Køge ash achieves the lowest strength, both in terms of the mean value and spread.

4. Results and Discussion
4.2.2
10% Substitution
The measured compressive strength and normalised strength with samples containing 10%
substitution, are plotted in Figure 4.2.4 and Figure 4.2.5 as a function of the curing time.
The measured compressive strengths, show the same results as for 5% substitution. However
it also emphasizes them further.
Where the mortar containing 5% silica fume reached 100% reference strength before 7 days,
samples containing 10% achieves the same at approx. 7 days. As for the 5% substitution,
the normalised strength remains roughly constant at 1 and 3 days of curing, followed by
a steep increase in slope. Unlike the 5% substitution, the steep increase does not subside
after 7 days of hydration, resulting in a normalised strength of 115% instead of 110% after
14 days of curing. Similar to the 5% substitution, the steep slope, subsides, resulting in a
shifting negative and positive slope between 28 and 90 days of curing. However, the decrease
in normalised strength is not as severe, as the reference samples are unable to achieve as
high a strength.
In the case of coal ﬂy ash substitution, the reference strength is reached just after 40 days
of curing, instead of 48. Were the 5% replacement strength ﬂuctuated around the reference
strength at 60 and 90 days of curing, the 10% strength is clearly shown to increase beyond
the reference. In terms of the normalised plot this is seen as an almost continuously increas-
ing positive slope. The point where the normalised strength, drastically increases, occurs
at 7 days of curing instead of 14. Although this is the case, it is worth noting that with
10% replacement, the early age strength is lower, than with 5% replacement. The plots thus
clearly demonstrates the improved properties of concrete with increased amount of cement
replacement.
As with the 5% substitution, the wood ashes and tailings shows no signs of improving the
strength of the mortar. Instead, the compressive strength is reduced further. It is shown in
two ways. Firstly as an overall lower strength at the diﬀerent stages of curing, and secondly
as a diﬀerence to the other 2 additives, especially the coal ﬂy ash. With the coal ﬂy ash
it is quite easily discernible, how the distance to the wood ash and tailing samples grows,
as the pozzolanic reaction transpires. The normalised compressive strength plot shows a
similar development as the 5% substitution. Similarly, the initial strength is low with the
tailings, between 75 and 80%, and increases to between 80 and 85%, while the tailings start
out with a high strength, this is especially the case for Køge ash, and ends at approx. 85%.
Concerning the curve for Amager ash, a few dips in strength are visible at around 28 and 60
days of curing. It is assumed that the strength would be closer to that of the tailings. The
decreases seen could be caused by the aforementioned changes in workability. Interestingly,
the curve for Køge ash was not as prone to drops in strength, and for the most part follows
a normal curve development. This could imply, that of the 4 alternative additives, the Køge
ash hampers the potential strength gain the most.

4. Results and Discussion
The measured compressive strengths with 10% substitution are depicted in Figure 4.2.6 as
a bar plot. The diﬀerences in strength are further emphasised here as well. It is again easily
discernible how samples with silica fume has a signiﬁcantly increased strength compared to
the reference. However, the coal ﬂy ash is also increased beyond that of the reference, and
has a standard deviation that lies within the range of the spread for silica fume, implying
that a substitution with coal ﬂy ash is capable of reaching similar values as the silica fume
samples. With 5% replacement, the standard deviation of the alternative additives, was
roughly the same as the reference. With 10% the gap is signiﬁcantly increased, with a stan-
dard deviation that does not reach that of the reference. Again, Køge ash is shown to reach
a slightly lower strength, with a lower spread as well. Additionally, the standard deviation
of the Zinkgruvan samples exceed the spread of Amager ash, implying the ability to surpass
it in terms of strength.
The testing indicates, that Køge ash has the poorest performance in terms of long term
properties. This, is understandable, given its large particle size, and potentially high or-
ganic content. Amager ash could potentially work as ﬁller, which could explain the slightly
higher mean strength. However, this is potentially superseded by its poor workability, where
Zinkgruvan instead performs better. Comparing the compressive strength of the 4 alterna-
tive additives with that of the 2 pozzolanes, it can be surmised that no noticeable, pozzolanic
reaction occurs, supporting the material assessments.
Parts of the previous research done showed similar results as the measured compressive
strengths. With the similar tailings of the article [8] it was determined, that the addition
of the tailings decreased the compressive strength regardless of the curing time or substitu-
tion amount. It was further determined, that the addition of water reducing agents did not
improve the overall strength. Additionally, the research in the article by Elbey et al.[16].
showed that their gold tailings did not reach similar strengths as silica fume or coal ﬂy ash
samples.
The article by Cheah and Ramli [10], that analysed a high calcium ﬂy ash, found that
the compressive strength decreased as well compared to a reference, during a curing period
of 90 days. This was partially also the case with results shown in the article utilization of
wood ash in concrete manufacturing [36]. Parts of the results displayed showed a decrease
in strength during a 90 day curing period, while other results showed an increase in strength
after a year.

4. Results and Discussion
4.2.2.1
Early Age Strength
Previously, the early age strength has only been partly discussed. The measured compres-
sive strengths at 1 and 3 days of curing are displayed as bar plots in the following ﬁgures.
Figure 4.2.7 and Figure 4.2.8 show the strength after 1 day for 5 and 10% substitution
respectively. After 1 day of curing, none of the mortars with additives achieve a strength
equal to that of the reference. Furthermore, in the case of 5% substitution, mortar with
Køge ash achieves the highest strength among the additives, both in mean value and stan-
dard deviation. It is not equal to the reference. At 10% substitution, silica fume achieves
a higher strength, followed by Køge ash. The ash was shown to have a large particle size
distribution. Therefore, the higher strength measurements is considered, as part of a ﬁller
eﬀect. Instead, this could be caused by some accelerative action, from the ashes chemical
composition. As previously mentioned, results from a previous project [32], showed that
mortar with Køge ash had a faster initial and ﬁnal setting time. Although, it would appear
to give a higher strength than the other additives, it thus not make up for the removed
cement. This is evidenced by the 10% substitution, where the strength is lowered further.
Of the other additives, coal ﬂy ash has the highest mean compressive strength, with 5%
substitution. The range of the standard deviation is the same as the silica fume sample.
This could indicate that both have the same eﬀectiveness as ﬁllers. Although, with 10% re-
placement, the strength of the coal ﬂy ash samples decreases, while the samples containing
silica fume remained with the same range. This is due to silica fumes smaller particle size
and its eﬀectiveness as ﬁller.
Amager ash was shown to have a similar particle distribution as coal ﬂy ash.
It would
therefore likely have a similar ﬁller eﬀect. This is conﬁrmed by the strength measurements.
At 5% substitution, less compressive strength were measured. The strengths are however,
close to one another, and at 10%, the mean strength measured is equal. The standard de-
viation of Amager ash is higher.
The mortar with tailing substitution, lack strength compared to the rest. This is due to the
large particle size of the tailings and a poor ﬁller eﬀect. Therefore the lack of cement has
greater eﬀect. This is shown with the diﬀerence between 5 and 10% substitution. It is worth
noting that it is Nalunaq that has the largest measured strength, and the smallest particle
size of the two tailings.

4. Results and Discussion
The compressive strength after 3 days of curing are included in Figure 4.2.9 and Fig-
ure 4.2.10, for 5 and 10% substitution respectively. At this point in the curing, the tailings
no longer lack behind the other additives, as previously seen. This could indicate that the
earlier lack of cement does not hinder the later strength as much as after 1 day of hydration.
In fact, the lowest strength measured is with Køge ash substitution. After its earlier lead it
falls behind both at 5 and 10% substitution. This is due to the earlier acceleration, hindering
further strength gain slightly, and the reason for its poorer performance over all.
After 3 days of hydration, in the case of 5% substitution, it is instead Amager ash, that
achieves the highest strength among the additives. This is likely due to some form of ac-
celeration as well. It is also possible that this is just how it was measured, as the standard
deviation of the mortar with nNlunaq falls with in the same range as Amager ash. At 10%
substitution the strength is below that of silica fume and coal ﬂy ash.
At 5% substitution the silica fume and coal ﬂy ash mortar has an equal strength.
At
10% substitution the mortar with silica fume does not loose any strength further, due to its
ﬁller eﬀect and quick pozzolanic reaction. Coal ﬂy ash achieves a slightly higher strength
than that of the alternative additives.
67

==PAGE:82==
4. Results and Discussion
4.3 |
Thermogravimetric Analysis and Hydration
The TGA and DSC measurements are located in Appendix E. The measurements show
the characteristic peaks, as discussed in the articles [6] and [7]. An initial peak, can be
seen at around 100°C , this is attributed to the C −S −H gel formed in the samples, that
along with other volatile matter is slowly reduced during the measurements. The peaks
seen between 400-500°C is due to the dehydration of Ca(OH)2 and those between 700 and
800°C are due to the added CaCO3 undergoing decarbonation. Among all the measurements
done, the highest mass change recorded between 700 and 800°C , is with samples containing
Køge ash. This can be attributed to its higher carbonate content. Although, the measured
carbonate content in Amager ash, was high as well, it does show the same level of mass
loss.
Interestingly, the measurements containing Zinkgruvan does almost experience the
same mass change, albeit with a much lower carbonate content. It is worth noting that a
signiﬁcantly lower mass loss is measured as well, increasing the standard deviation. The
residual mass measured, decreases with increasing hydration. This is due to the increasing
C −S −H gel and portlandite content in the samples. The total mass loss is highest for the
samples containing wood ash, and lowest for the samples with tailings. The increased mass
loss of the samples with wood ash, could be from both the increased carbonate content, and
unburned organic matter. The increased residual mass of the samples containing tailings is
likely due to the tailings inactivity.
The measured calcium hydroxide amount, at the diﬀerent points of hydration is depicted in
Figure 4.3.1 and Figure 4.3.2 for 5 and 10% cement replacement. The measurement amount
is shown here, as outlined in Section 2.6. The highest amount of Ca(OH)2 measured is
roughly 11 wt%, belonging to the Zinkgruvan samples. The fact that the highest measure-
ment is not achieved with the reference is puzzeling, in the case of substitution, the wt% of
Ca(OH)2 should decrease with increased substituion. This is evidenced by the visible de-
cline in Ca(OH)2 from 5 to 10% substitution. The fact that the highest measurement is 11
wt.%, raises a few questions. According to Mehta and Monteiro [22], the amount of calcium
hydroxide formed is around 20-25%, while other sources such as [7] found the portlandite
content to be 16.8% after 48 days of hydration. The low amount measured is therefore per-
plexing. One rational could be the addition of limestone ﬁller in the basis cement used. As
mentioned in the previous section, drops in mass, was identiﬁed at around 700°C belonging
to CaCO3. With additional mass of CaCO3, the wt% of Ca(OH)2 would again be reduced.
Another possible reason could be leaching. Although, the cement paste preparation method
voids bleeding, it is possible that when the pastes are placed in water, that the portlandite
partly dissolves in to the water. This is evidenced by the small decline in Ca(OH)2 of the
reference sample after 28 days of hydration. A third possibility is that thermogravimetric
analysis, as a method, is mostly eﬀective at early age hydration. Even with a lower amount
then expected, several deductions can still be made from the measurements.
69

==PAGE:84==
4. Results and Discussion
As with the compression strength plots the extreme pozzolanicity of silica fume is quite
apparent. Of all the additives the silica fume curve achieves the lowest content of Ca(OH)2.
This is easily discernible, with 10% substitution, where the gap between the curves have
increased signiﬁcantly. This can be an indicator of silica fumes quick hydration, limiting
the amount of Ca(OH)2 formed. In the case of 5% substitution the maximum Ca(OH)2 is
reached after 14 days of curing, while with 10% replacement this is achieved after 3 days,
and marked by a large drop in the portlandite content. The decrease is then marked by a
slight increase. The small increase is considered as measurement uncertainty. For both 5
and 10% substitution, the Ca(OH)2 formed decreases slowly after 28 days.
With coal ﬂy ash replacement, the Ca(OH)2 content increases up until 28 days, after which
the content decreases. This appears to be the case for both 5 and 10% replacement. The
decrease appear more pronounced with 10% replacement than fore 5% replacement.
At
10% substitution the decrease occurs before 28 days, with a lower content at 60 days of
curing. This corroborates the compression strengths measured. It was observed, that due
to the slow pozzolanic reaction, the strength increased beyond the reference after 28 days of
hydration. Lilkov et al. [7] found that mineral additions lowered the portlandite content to
11.3-13.5%. The content measured here is signiﬁcantly lower for both, following the previ-
ously mentioned reasons.
The wood ash curves are less promising in comparison. In regards to the 10% substitu-
tion, both curves appear to increase and remain constant like the reference curve. Both
curves appear to follow the same development.
A higher Ca(OH)2 has been measured
during the early age for the samples containing Amager Ash, than samples with Køge ash.
This is the case for both 5 and 10% substitution, with the exception of the 5% replacement
after 1 day. The measured Ca(OH)2 content is signiﬁcantly lower in comparison. In the
case of 5% substitution, the two curves ﬂuctuates at a higher rate. For both 5 and 10%
substitution at ﬁrst a rise in Ca(OH)2 content is visible. This is followed by a decrease
upon reaching 60 days. The subsequent measurements at 90 days shows an increase, once
more negating the possibility of pozzolanicity. After 1 day the Ca(OH)2 content of the
samples with Køge ash substitution is higher than the reference. It is hard to determine if
this is due to the Ca(OH)2 in the ash or a form of acceleration, as all the other additives,
with the exception of Amager ash and silica fume, achieve the same. Although, after 3
days Amager ash achieves a higher Ca(OH)2 content then the reference. Although it had
the highest measured compressive strength, this was not enough to overtake the reference
strength.
71
==PAGE:85==
4. Results and Discussion
The tailings, like the wood ash curves, do not indicate any potential pozzolanic reaction
occurring. In the case of 5% substitution, both curves follow the reference curve closely.
Zinkgruvan, gave approximately the same values as for the reference, albeit with a decrease
at 7 days, this is considered a measurement uncertainty as well. After 28 days the amount
of Ca(OH)2 decreases, same as for the reference, thus suﬀering from the same errors. 10%
substitution follows the same development, although with an overall lower Ca(OH)2 content,
with an even more pronounced decrease at 7 days of hydration. With the slowly decreasing
content in portlandite, it would appear that Zinkgruvan tailings possesses some pozzolanic
abilities, however due to its characterisation, and the similar decrease with the reference,
it would seem unlikely. Compared to the decrease seen with coal ﬂy ash, the decrease due
to Zinkgruvan seems insigniﬁcant. As mentioned, Nalunaq follows the reference closely as
well. Decreases in Ca(OH)2 do occur at 28 and 60 days of curing, likewise as for the wood
ashes. This is considered a measurement error as well, as the 90 day measurement shows
an increase. It can therefore be further surmised, that the substitution with tailings does
not improve the properties of concrete in the long term. This is opposite the ﬁndings of
Yunhong et al. [8], they concluded, that the addition of tailings consumed the Ca(OH)2 in
a secondary hydration reaction. Although, the tailings used are fundamentally diﬀerent, the
tailings from the article by Yunhon et al. [8] share a similar chemical composition.
72
==PAGE:86==
4. Results and Discussion
4.4 |
Compressive Strength: Substitution & Workability
The measured compressive strength with increasing additive substitution amount, is depicted
in Figure 4.4.1. The eﬀect of increasing substitution amount is quite clear. In regards to
the coal ﬂy ash, an increase in substitution amount does appear to impede the compressive
strength of the mortar. As previously shown, at 5% replacement, the compressive strength
is almost the same magnitude as the reference sample, and exceeds the reference, at 10%
replacement. A further increase in cement replacement, would appear to ﬁrst lower the
strength, then increase it again. A possible explanation could be linked to the consistency
of the mortar. Figure 4.4.2 depicts the ﬂow value, measured according to DS/EN 1015-
3:1999. The initial substitution with coal ﬂy ash appears to have lowered the consistency,
while further replacement improves upon this. With a higher workability at 20 and 30%
the resulting strength would improve as well, thus achieving the same strength and more,
than the reference. Examining the standard deviation of the results reveal an increase in
deviation for 20 and 30% replacement. At 5 and 10% the standard deviation is approx. 1.6
%, at 20 and 30% the deviation is 5.02 and 3.47% respectively. It would therefore seem,
that the same strength can be achieved with either a 20 or 30% substitution, albeit with
larger variations.
Likewise, as previously shown, a replacement of 5 and 10% cement with silica fume, in-
creases the 90 day strength of the cement. Compared to coal ﬂy ash a further increase in
substitution does not beneﬁt the mechanical strength as much. At 20% substitution, the
compressive strength is approx. the same as the reference, while an increase to 30% sub-
stitution, decreases the compressive strength to less than 30 MPa. Comparing Figure 4.4.1
with, Figure 4.4.2, it can be surmised that the eventual decrease in strength are coupled
with large decreases in the consistency. In fact the ﬂow value appear to almost decrease at
a linear rate. Although the strength does increase with 10 % replacement, the consistency
would appear to have dropped roughly 15%, and roughly 30% with 30% substitution. It is of
course noted, that the use of superplasticers is advised, when working with silica fume [38].
Furthermore, it can be noted that the 20% replacement standard deviation is quite large,
compared to the rest. It is due to the fact that, if the mixture is mixed well, a large strength
can be achieved, higher than for 10% substitution. Due to the decrease in workability this
is not easily so.

==PAGE:88==
4. Results and Discussion
Contrary to the coal ﬂy ash and silica fume, the two wood ashes and tailings shows no
improvement in strength, when substituting 5 and 10% of the cement, as previously illus-
trated. Figure 4.4.1 illustrates further how similar the measured compressive strengths are.
It is easy to discern how tangled the measured compressive strengths are at 5% and 10%
substitution. Nevertheless, that is where the similar decrease in strength ends. Interestingly,
a stepwise increase in replacement with the tailings, slightly lowers the strength. The two
tailings appear to have a near identical development. Implying, that although diﬀerent,
the addition of the two tailings has the same eﬀect. Furthermore, the decrease in strength
appear closely linear, implying that the mortar strength is not aﬀected by the addition of
tailings other than lowering the cement ratio. Based on Figure 4.4.2, it can be surmised
that the addition of tailings does not hamper the consistency of the mortar. The addition
of Nalunaq tailings, decreases the ﬂow value approx. 7%, and remains almost constant at
such a level, no matter the substitution amount. Zinkgruvan is shown, at ﬁrst to increase
the consistency and then slowly decrease it with increasing replacement. The diﬀerences
in workability, are among other, caused by the diﬀerences in particle size. Although, the
consistency is lowered, and thus water requirement increased, it would appear not to impede
the workability. This is also the case for 5 and 10% substitution with wood ash. Although,
further increase in replacement has severe consequences.
Køge ash shows the same near linear decrease. The loss of strength is quite severe in compar-
ison. At 30% substitution, the compressive strength of the mortar is roughly 10 MPa. This
is also the case of Amager ash. The strength loss is even lower, and follows a more parabolic
development. The compressive strength is shown to decrease below 10 MPa already at 20%
replacement and only further decreases to approx. 5% at 30% substitution. The increased
loss of compressive strength can be explained by the consistency of the mortar. At 20 and
30% replacement, the ﬂow value drops below what is measurable by the test format used.
Amager ash loses strength at a much higher rate compared to Køge ash, although not mea-
surable with the test used, it would imply that Amager ash has a more severe eﬀect on the
workability, than Køge ash. It is worth noting that the loss in consistency is greater, for
the wood ashes, than silica fume. This would imply that the loss of workability is greater
with wood ashes, without any of the positive beneﬁts of silica fume. The article by Raheem
et al [13]. suggest that an increase beyond 10% replacement with wood ash increases the
workability, in the same manner as the coal ﬂy ash shown here, requiring less water. The
ﬁndings of Abdullahi [12], showed an increase in water requirements in order to sustain the
same level of workability as the reference, similarly as shown here.
75
==PAGE:89==
==PAGE:90==
5
Conclusion
The material assessment of the 6 additives revealed, that the wood ashes and tailings pos-
sessed both similar as well as widely diﬀerent characteristics. When compared to that of
the coal ﬂy ash and silica fume, the 4 alternative additives proved lacking. The assessment
showed that the wood ashes possessed a high pH, with potential beneﬁts in concrete. They
where however shown to possess a high amount of calcium carbonate, and in the case of
Køge ash a high amount of unburned organic matter as well. The wood ashes was shown to
have a high amount of water soluble anions and cations, where the tailings silica fume and
coal ﬂy ash possessed little.
The chemical content of element measurements, revealed a high amount of Ca in the wood
ashes, coupled with a low amount of Si. The analysis also showed a high amount in the
tailings and an even higher amount in the coal ﬂy ash and silica fume. This indicated a poor
pozzolanic performance of the wood ashes. The X-ray diﬀraction showed several compounds
containing Si in the tailings. Additionally, it was hypothesised due to the tailings origin,
that they possessed little to no amorphous silicate. The wood ashes was found to contain
several salts that together with CaCO3 could serve as accelerators. The particle size dis-
tributions, revealed that the tailings and Køge ash would have a poor performance as ﬁller,
while Amager ash was shown to have similar distribution to coal ﬂy ash, and potential ﬁller
capabilities.
The measured compressive strengths as a function of curing time, showed the strength
improving capabilities of the coal ﬂy ash and silica fume. The eﬀect of wood ash and tail-
ings on a concretes properties were clariﬁed as well. The addition of either wood ash or
tailings decreased the compressive strength of the mortar, at all points of curing measured.
Unlike coal ﬂy ash and silica fume an increase in cement replacement did not increase the
strength, but decreased it further instead. Although the wood ashes and tailings are funda-
mentally diﬀerent, similar strength was achieved. It was therefore surmised, that the wood
ashes or tailings does not serve as pozzolans or improves the long term properties of concrete.
The early age testing showed diﬀerent results. The 5% cement replacement measurement
showed, that Køge ash gave the highest strength of the 6 additives after 1 day of curing. The
measured strength is not greater than the reference strength. With increased replacement, it
is silica fume that achieves the highest strength next to the reference, due to a stronger ﬁller
eﬀect. With a similar particle distribution, Amager ash and coal ﬂy ash achieved similar
strengths, while the use of tailings resulted in a signiﬁcant decrease in strength. After 3
77
==PAGE:91==
5. Conclusion
days of curing Amager ash achieved the highest strength among the additives. The higher
strength from the wood ashes, can in part be due to a ﬁller eﬀect, and an acceleration of
the setting times and hardening.
The ﬂow tests done, showed a decrease in mortar workability, with increased cement sub-
stitution for all additives except coal ﬂy ash. The loss of workability of the mortar with
tailings was not as severe, as with wood ash and silica fume. Furthermore, it was shown that
the workability of mortar with wood ash were worse than silica fume addition. The test was
coupled compression testing, whereby it could be surmised that the decrease in workability
when using tailings, did not eﬀect the compressive strength of the mortar. The compressive
strength was however, deeply decreased when using wood ash. The result indicated that
the addition had a worse eﬀect on the workability than silica fume, with any of the positive
beneﬁts.
The thermogravimetric analysis done showed the expected phases in cement paste sam-
ples.
The measured Ca(OH)2 content was lower than expected.
However, even with a
lower Ca(OH)2 content, several deduction could still be made. The eﬃciency of silica fume
addition was easily discernible, due to its sizeable decrease in Ca(OH)2 compared to the
reference. The addition of coal ﬂy ash decreased the Ca(OH)2 content as well, although to
a lesser extent. The addition of the 4 alternative additives did not decrease the Ca(OH)2
content. It was therefore surmised further, that no pozzolanic reaction was occurring, and
their addition did not improve the strength properties.
The addition of wood ash and tailing does not signiﬁcantly improve the properties of con-
crete in the long term, while the wood ash could potentially improve the short term prop-
erties.
78
==PAGE:92==
5. Conclusion
5.1 |
Suggestions for Future Research
The investigations performed as part of this thesis has provided a lot of clariﬁcation, leading
to several conclusions on the properties of concrete with wood ash and mine tailing addi-
tives. The focus was on both the short term and long term properties. The research showed
a potential eﬀect during the early stages of hydration with wood ash substitution. Focusing
on ﬁrst to seventh day of hydration could provide additional insight. Additionally it could
be beneﬁcial to investigate the ﬁrst 24 hours of hydration thoroughly.
As part of the theory chapter, the heat of hydration of curing cement paste was discussed.
Testing the heat of hydration of cement with wood ash and tailings could provide further
insight.
The assessment of the tailings revealed a similar particle size as the sand used. Investi-
gating the potential use of tailings as sand replacement appears advantageous.
It was shown that the workability was severely impaired with the addition of wood ash.
Changing the water ratio accordingly to achieve the same workability as the reference is an-
other potential research point. Additionally, the use of water reducing agents are suggested
when adding silica fume during mix design. Analysing the eﬀect of water reducing agents
with wood ash adds to the potential.
On a ﬁnal note, thermogravimetric analysis was chosen to analyse the cement hydration
instead of x-ray diﬀraction. Repeating the tests using x-ray diﬀraction is a possible source
of insight into the properties of wood ash and tailings as well.

C
Compression testing results
The ensuing sections contains the compression test results for the reference mortar batches
and substitution mortar batches.
Symbol
Enhed
Betydning
A
[mm2]
Area of compression.
F1
[g]
Result of ﬁrst compression test.
F2
[g]
Result of second compression test.
Fg
[g]
Mean of ﬁrst and second compression tests.
FG
[g]
Mean compression test result for a batch.
σ
[g]
Compressive strength of a batch.
σX
[g]
Standard deviation of a batch.
